I Dr. M. PEEEY,
M.E.C.S., L.S.A , Ac.,
SPECIALIST for the Treatment of Nervous
Complaints, Diabetes, Tumors, &c.
Registered by the Medical Board of N.S. W. Late House Sur-
geon to the Great Northern Hospital, London
I am aware that I am not strictly following the paths
Of professional etiquette by advertising- myself as a
Specialist for the treatment of this class of disease, but,
Unfortunately for the suffering public in Australia, the
apathy shown by Qualified Men in the treatment of
Chronic and Nervous Complaints has induced men of the
lowest grade of intellect (whose only qualifications are
unlimited assurance) to spring up, and by specious
advertisements and false promises trap the unwary.
It is very evident that sufferers from these diseases
have been scandalously treated by these unqualified
persons The evidence taken at the Medical Enquiry
held in Sydney (published in the Daily Til graph, Nov.
6th 7th and Sth, lSln>) plainly shows the disgraceful
manner in wlfch patients are duped. Not only are
they robbed of their money, but what is of infinitely
greater importance, their health is injured also. It is
therefore time that Legally Qualified Men entered the
field to fight these pests of society with their own
weapons, viz., Advertising.
It is utterly impossible for those who are suffering
from the e diseases, who are scattered over nil parts of
Australasia, to ascertain which Qualified i’raclUiouer
makes these diseases his speciality unless he advertises.
I consider therefore, that by advertising I not only
strike a blow at the Quackery which now exists, hut I also add
to the usefulness of my profession and in no way lessen my
personal dignity.
My (Jaaliflcatlon* I invite the fullest Investigation into. For some considerable time X was House Surgeon to the
Groat Northern Hospital, London, where I had special opportunities of oi servin ; the many plioses of the Nervous and
Sexual System, and to heroine acquainted with the most scientific methods of effecting their cure.
My sole attention is given to the treatment of these diseases, and when a person consults me he is informed at ihe
commencement whether I can effect his cure or not. The patient is not deluded with false and specious promises, and
if I under t dee the case
I Absolutely Guarantee a Complete and Permanent Cure.
My great success in the treatment of these diseases enables me to give this guarantee without fear.

UNCLEAN LEGISLATORS.
“ When commenting recently upon the position in which the O’Shea
disclosures placed Mr. Parnell we expressed satisfaction at the giowth
of a public opinion which refused to accept public sen ices, however
eminent, in eondoneraent of criminal personal conduct. . . . And
public opinion is perfectly right. .... The public verdict in the
cases of Dilke and Parnell emphatically assorts that morality must in
future be as essential to public life as ability, and that the absence of
the one cannot be compensated for by the presence of the other. Ihe
strong public sentiment which in England lately has been pronouncing
inexorable ostracism against transgressors ,of tie moral code is based
on an increased understanding of .ethical morality,. The feeling which
underlies it is not only abhorrence of criminality per se, but a right
apprehension of the fact that one who is guilty of private transgression
cannot be trusted with the conscientious discharge of public duties.
. . , . It is a pretty safe maxim that whoever will deal selfishly
and dishonestly with his fellow-men ?is individuals will do the tame
with them as a whole when the opportunity presents itself. To place
in positions of supreme trust as law-makers and custodians of public
finances men who cannot in .their own personal affairs present a clean
record is about the same as to choose bank-managers from among
known pickpockets The doors of Parliament should stand
firmly closed against the trickster whose career has led him to them
through a course of dishonest a,nd dubious actions . . . and to the
foul and lecherous creature whose domestic conduct is a blot and a dis-
grace upon the society which his presence pollutes. • • • • We are
not so short of men or of manhood in N.S. Wales as to be forced to
seek law-makers in the ranks of hypocritical . . . adulterers. The
people must insist more than they have yet done upon getting repre-
sentatives conduct is able to bear daylight upon it in every par-
ticular either in or out of Parliament. _ We want honest and trust-
worthy men to make our laws, not salacious reprobates .... or
unscrupulous scoundrels.” — Sydney Ministerial Organ, 2Gth Feb.,
1891.
THE STRAIGHT TIP.
PARRES (reading Sydney D. T. leader); “Quite right. My word, we must put all such people out of Parliament."

SATURDAY, MARCH 7, 1891.
Commercial Depression.
For several years past the world of alleged
civilisation has been suffering from a bad
attack of Commercial Depression. Man is
at present desperately hard-up; He is only
a shade lower than the angels, but to a large
extent he is sleeping in boilers on the wharf.
The disease from which he is suffering is a
species of unaccountable and impalpable
curse, which comes from no visible place,
and arises from no visible source, and is
governed by no recognised laws, and which
goes as fitfully and as mysteriously as it
comes. It is generally alluded to as dullness
of trade, though why trade is dull no one
can tell ; there are upwards of a dozen con-
tradictory explanations of the phenomenon,
and they are all about equally unreasonable.
But regarding the result there can be no
manner of doubt ; from China to Peru and
from the Cape of Good Hope to Archangel the
worker is hard-up, and work is scarce,
and wages are tending downward, and
the unemployed, who are numerous
even in periods of alleged prosperity, are
very much more numerous in this period of
conspicuous adversity. The curse of com-
mercial depression is everywhere, save in
those happy lands where there is no com-
merce to be depressed, and no rent, and no
landlords, and no employers, and no capital,
and where the blessed savage prospects for
fish and bananas, and enjoys the sunlight in
happy ignorance of the fact that trade is
falling off. Yet even there the ominous
preacher is busy spreading civilisation, and
teaching the native how to be thrown out by
his landlord when he is sick and can’t raise
the money for the rent, and how to be run
in by the policeman when he is hard-up, and
how to be sent to gaol by the magistrate
when he doesn’t “ keep off the grass,” and
in a general way he is conferring on the
untutored aborigine the blessings of com-
mercial depression. But as to the remedy
of this mysterious evil, the wastes and
deserts of orthodoxy have never yet grown
any specific which promises the slightest
relief ; it is vaguely assumed that depression
comes by the act and dispensation of Puovi-
I'KNCB, which is another way of assuming
that Providence is depressed, and there the
matter rests.
# # #
That wildly uncertain influence known as
over-production is, however, the cause most
commonly ascribed when the world is a little
more conspicuously hard-up than usual.
That is to say, that the world produces too
much wealth in the shape of food, clothing,
find alj other kinds of necessaries and
luxuries, and because it has produced too
much wealth, therefore it becomes poor. A
presumably sane and intelligent population
iccepts this as a perfectly reasonable theory,
owing chiefly to that dismal ignorance that
Makes every population believe whatever it
is told to believe, if the statement is only
loud enough and made with sufficient energy.
No doubt if it was announced that any single
individual was starving through having made
too much money, or had been burnt to
death through falling up a cataract, there
would be a general impression that some-
thing had gone wrong ; but when it tran-
spires that three-fourths of civilised huma-
nity are in terrible straits through having
created too much riches, the fact is ac-
cepted as a natural and inevitable state
of things which must not be questioned,
for fear of irritating Providence. If
the world produced too much goods, then
it ought, in all reason, to be too rich instead
of too poor; the man who suffers from
over-production either of money or of
money’s worth should be a millionaire in-
stead of a pauper ; but the unemployed and
unshod shoemaker is in his present lament-
able condition because the world has too
many boots, and when it has too many boots
somehow it can’t get boots ; and the tattered
tailor, who is out of a job, is short of clothes
because of the over-production of clothes.
And in like manner the starving agricul-
turist is hungry and empty because of the
superfluity of food—in fact, the whole theory
of over-production is a plan by which the
effect shoves the cause backward, and the
consequence abolishes its own origin—a
scheme whereby the worker is poor when he
produces little, and poorer still if he pro-
duces much, and if he produces very much
he is then poorest of all. In short, the
assertion that the working masses are
reduced to extreme misery by the over-
production of wealth in any form—whether
as money or as goods—is not only a glaring
lie ; it is also as ghastly an absurdity as the
assertion that thirst arises from the glut of
fresh water, or that drowning is generally
brought about by the pressure of too much
dry land.
* * *
The misery that prevails among the poor
in times of commercial depression arises
simply from the fact that the worker is the
only individual in the whole range of
humanity who has no recognised right to
exist except when his employer has a profit-
able use for him—in his own individual
capacity he has no right to exist at all. In
the intervals of feverish prosperity which
precede most periods of scarcity, the toiler is
urged on to produce in three years enough
to supply the world’s requirements for five,
and when the day of glutted markets arrives
he is sent adrift. A horse, in such a case, is
turned out to grass ; a human being is
turned out without the grass. If the owner
of the horse, in a period of commercial de-
pression, ejects his steed to starve on a
macadamised road, the animal is not said to
be suffering from over-production ; he
is suffering from the inhumanity of
his proprietor, and that individual is
promptly fined or sent to gaol ; and
the virtual owper of the man is not
tined in like manner simply because the
horse has a right to live somewhere or some-
how, whether his services are required or
not, and the human employe has no such
right. He has produced wealth—too much
wealth according to the grand old economic
theory—for his owner ;he has received no
share of that wealth, except what was neces-
sary to keep him in working condition for
his employer’s benefit ; and then he is thrown
out till he is wanted again. If he is dead
before he is wanted again his owner can get
another man without any extra charge. And
in the intervals of spasmodic production the
toiler occupies a strange position, for he has
no right to live without working, and he has
no right to work till the capitalist has a new'
demand for him ; he has no right to live on
the land because it belongs to the landlord,
and he has no right to loaf on the street
because the policeman bids him “ move on ”
—in fact, he has absolutely no rights ot any
kind apart from his employer. He is a victim
of circumstances certainly, but not in the com-
monly accepted sense ; it is not customary,
for instance, to say that the individual who is
run down by a furious driver is crushed by
the over-production of cabs, or that the per-
son who is eaten by a cannibal is a victim of
the inevitable over-production of hungry
aborigines. He merely sutlers from the fact
that a stony system of monopoly has made it
impossible for a man without capital to earn
his livelihood except at the pleasure and con-
venience of an employer, who chooses at
times to make him produce too much and
refuses at other times to let him produce at
all, just as he thinks fit; and if this is suffer-
ing through over-production and consequent
depression of trade, then the man who is
drowning in a well, and is dependent for his
means of getting out on the man who stands
on the top with a rope, is in trouble from
exactly the same cause. But if the man at
the top finds it more profitable not to lower
(he rope, that circumstance is not generally
called depression of trade ; the law- uses
strong language in such a case, and resorts
to strong measures.
* # #
This simile is not in any sense strained or
overdrawn, for in every country which is
overshadowed by the curso of civilisation the
man without capital is absolutely and utterly
dependent on his employer. He cannot
resort to the land for a livelihood, for it is
the property of the landlord; he cannot start
a store or a workshop, for the ground it is
built upon must be paid for ; he cannot fish
or hunt without trespassing on private pro-
perty —he can do nothing in fact, save work
for some capitalist, for a thousand different
monopolies have combined to crush out every
opportunity which once existed by which
any man devoid of property could earn an
independent livelihood. And, therefore, the
men who hold and enjoy these monopolies
are morally bound to find employment for
the whole human race, as the only possible
reason or excuse for the privileges which they
enjoy. So far, however, they have succeeded,
by the great principle of shifting the responsi-
bility and passing on the blame, in dodging
this gigantic liability. In a country, for in-
stance, where there are ten million people,
and ten thousand monopolists appropriate
among them the land, money, machinery,
and every other source of livelihood, it is
difficult to fix the exact responsibility, and
exceedingly hard to hang the right mono-
polist ; yet for every individual who dies of
want and misery in the slums of London or
Glasgow or New York, some monopolist
ought, while capital punishment exists, cer-
tainly to be hanged if he could only be
found. If instead of ten thousand capitalists
and landholders possessing ail the resources
of ten million people there were one thou-
sand inhabitants planted, without any possi-
bility of escape upon an island, where one
solitary plutocrat owned the land, the houses,
money, machinery, fisheries, and all other
industry, the question would be easy of solu-
tion. The great law that he who will not
work must perish would remain in force just
the same, but when only one man could
supply the work he must either do so or
abdicate his position, or be deposed, or
hanged. His proposition of the aggregate
profit and the aggregate responsibility
would be as before, and his right to re-
fuse employment and allow the other inha-
bitants to starve by reason of over-produc-
tion, or depression of trade, would remain
unimpaired ; but if he failed to provide em-
ployment, and yet made it impossible for
anyone else to provide it, his funeral would
be very close at hand, and no doctrine
of political economy could stop it.
* # #
Yet this dull and ignorant world has not
yet realised that the monopolists, who have
made the whole race of workers dependent
on them by annexing the land and every
other source of wealth, are thereby bound to
find work and sustenance for the whole
human family, just as the monopolist who
seizes the only well in a thirsty desert is
morally bound to supply water to the other
inhabitants. Consequently it drivels at
banquets and complimentary feeds about the
virtues of the great land-stealer who has
“furnished employment” to a few of his
fellow-creatures, instead of braining the
land-stealer who does not furnish employ-
ment on a charge of attempted murder by
slow starvation ; and when the employment
gives out, and wages sink to zero, and star-
vation is everywhere, the result is set down
to over-production, or “depression of
trade.” Trade has now been depressed for
a good many years, yet the aggregate of the
world’s commerce has kepi on expanding as
it always does whether trade is depressed or
not. Also manufactures have continued to
grow, though not so rapidly as before, and
the dividends of banks and finance-com-
panies have risen almost as much as usual,
and shipping has multiplied ; in fact, depres-
sion simply means that monopolies have in-
creased slowly instead of increasing with
frenzied haste. And, as for rent, in the
aggregate it has gone up as usual, for rent
never stops by any human possibility. If
rents and land-values, dividends, reserve
funds, and all the other perquisites of capital
dropped in the same ratio as wagesj then the
difference between periods of commercial
prosperity and periods of adversity, so far as
the workers are concerned, would be hardly
perceptible. Some day, it may be, a great,
universal labour league and monopoly will
succeed in shifting the burden, and then the
first symptom of depression will be a reduc-
tion of rent instead of wages, and the useless
landlord will be locked-out instead of the
useful worker, and the term “ working half-
time ” will mean that the proprietor will
receive his rent for only three days in each
week, and then the toiler instead of the
gilded loafer will hold meetings of sympathy,
and urge the landlord to pray for better
times, and to bear up against the affliction
which arises out of the inscrutable ways of
Providence.
* # #
Such a change would be both a natural
and reasonable one, and yet this world will
be a seriously-astonished old globe when it
comes about. It has come to regard it as a
matter of course that the over-production of
wealth should leave the class which produced
thac wealth in a state of .utter and miserable
destitution, and when the human race in the
aggregate is suffering from a too great supply
of food and clothes and furniture, and similar
articles, it seems quite a reasonable result
that half the individual members of that
race should be unable to get any of
these articles, simply because of their
abundance. It considers it as part of the
unchangeable design of a presumably sane
Providence that in every period of tem-
porary scarcity the worker—the one in-
dividual whom the world cannot spare—
should bo sacrificed, while the landlord and
the monopolist—whom the world does not
want—go on as usual, and it is utterly
unable to realise that such a scheme is more
absurd than feeding a dog on kings when
meat is in short supply. And in particular
it actually believes that poverty and want
are caused by that mysterious influence
called “ depression of trade,” which is a truly
wonderful example of the power of vigorous
and vociferous perjury to disseminate any
theory, however glaringly ridiculous. If a
partnership of three men loses £lO,OOO out
of its joint capital of £300,000, and two of
the members judiciously convert this loss
into a profit by murdering the third and
annexing his share of the property, the third
man dies through a falling-off in trade in
- exactly the same way as hundred of workers
die every year, but in such cases this slow
old sphere wakes up and creates a dust. Yet
if the hangman were provided with a dic-
tionary and a brain he would probably be
surprised to find how little difference there
is between “commercial depression” and
murder after all.
Oswald Keatinge’s Conviction: Its
Democratic Moral.
The Bulletin has no desire to shed one tear
over the man who so tragically closed a
chequered career in Darlinghurst Prison last
week under the borrowed name of Oswald
Keatinge. There is no reasonable moral
ground for doubting that he did commit the
offence of which he was found guilty ; his his-
tory is of such a character as to preclude any
regrets from the point of view of the general
community at his withdrawal from the stage of
life, and as for the wretched old man himself,
he is far better off at rest for ever than with
five years’ penal servitude to add to his already
long and varied experience of prison life. But,
while not denying that Keatinge’s sentence
was probably well-deserved—that is ip say,
well-deserved to the extent that the same may
be said of perhaps 40 per cent, of the inmates
of Australian gaols--we would point out that
had he been a well-to-do man he would not
have died in Darlinghurst. Looking at the
published reports of the case, it is pretty plain
that the only reason he was found guilty was
that he had not money enough to brief a bar-
rister. The direct evidence of the perpetration
of the offence, apart from the testimony of the
little girl concerned—a child of 12—seems to
have been practically nil. Had Keating e stood
his trial with a clean record, had there been,
as is usual in criminal trials, no evidence of
previous convictions submitted against him in
advance, had the jury, in short, seen the
prisoner only as he appeared in the dock, or as
he was known to many Sydney people—the
Reverend Doctor Oswald Keatinge, quiet,
pleasant-mannered, .cultured gentleman, it is
almost impossible that they could have con-
sidered the case so free from reasonable doubt
as to have refused him an acquittal. No charge
is easier to make, no charge requires less evi-
dence for the establishment of a primd facie
ease, than one of the kind that Keatinge had
to answer; and there is no charge more difficult
to conclusively rebut. Any man whom chance
throws for the briefest interval into the com-
pany of one of the other sex, without the
presence of witnesses,- takes the risk of an
accusation of criminal assault, and should the
accusation be made, no matter how falsely, he
is fortunate if he can produce a scrap of evi-
dence to support his bare denial. It is a
knowledge of this fact that always makes a
decent jury very cautious about bringing in a
verdict of guilty on the unsupported statement
of the prosecutrix. A prisoner is almost sure
to escape if the respectability of his appearance
raises a presumption in his favour, and the testi-
mony against him is only the statement of a child,
without corroboration on any material points,
so long as no evidence is forthcoming to counter-
balance the original favourable presumption.
In Keatinge’s ease there can be little doubt
that what ruined his chance with the jury was
the disclosure of his previous disreputable his-
tory. But for that disclosure he might have
stepped from the dock a free man. Now, that
disclosure could not have been made if Keat-
inge had been defended by a sane barrister.
* * *
In a criminal case no evidence can be given as
to the character of a prisoner unless he himself
raises the question of character. Keatinge
appears to have asked one of the Crown wit-
nesses whether the latter had not heard of him
as a man of good repute, or something of that
sort. No lawyer who knew this client’s history
could have asked that innocent-looking question
because he would have foreseen the consequences.
It was evidence of character tendered by the
prisoner, and so enabled the prosecution to get
in all they knew to his discredit, not one word
of which would otherwise have been admissible.
Had Keatinge avoided that blunder, the jury
would have gone out to consider their verdict
without a suspicion that the man they were
trying had ever stood behind the spikes before,
and the chances are a hundred to one that the
verdict would have been “ not guilty.” It may
be said that that would have been a miscarriage
of justice. Perhaps it would. The Bulletin
has no fault to find with the thing called justice
for miscarrying—the point is that if it did
happen to carry straight on this occasion it was
only because it was aimed at a man with an
empty pocket.
# # *
Now, if criminal trials are to be conducted
fairly, the law on this point must be made one
thing or the other. If previous convictions and
conduct are to be put into the scale against one
man on his trial, then let every other man in
the same position, rich or poor, well-educated
or uninstructed, be treated in the same way.
Either the character and reputation of an
accused, are elements that ought to be taken into
account by a jury in deciding on guilt or
innocence, or they are not. If they are not,
then it is a scandalous injustice that ignorance
of a petty technicality of law should expose one
man to the disadvantage of having all his past
and legally-expiated sins raked up against him.
If the average jury were composed of skilled
psychologists, there -would be something to be
said in favour of supplying them with a detailed
history of the prisoner’s previous career, and
that of his immediate and collateral relations.
Twelve scientists deputed to decide whether
a given individual was guilty of putting
another man’s name at the bottom of a
cheque might be able to determine accurately


how far the probabilities of the case were
affected by the fact that his grandmother
suffered from a certain fixed delusion. They
could, perhaps, settle the precise presumption
of guilt to be fairly deduced from his previous
conviction for assaulting a policeman. For all
we know, the presumption in that case might
be the other way. But with scratch juries
who have not made a special study of the laws
of heredity and mental development, The
Bulletin believes in the common-sense arrange-
ment of trying a person charged with an offence
on evidence relating to that offence, and nothing
else. If there be evidence enough to convict
him, let him go to gaol for a longer or shorter
terra whether he has been there before or not ;
in the absence of that evidence, why should the
Crown be allowed to make up for the want of it
by raising a prejudice against him in the minds
of the jury? Just look at the thing from this
standpoint, dear reader : put yourself in the
dock with a charge of no matter what hanging
over your head. How would you like to lay
your past life absolutely bare to the Crown
Prosecutor, and let him quote just so much as
suited him, with a clever lawyer’s twist on it,
before the 12 men—men often ignorant and
brutal and prejudiced, who had your life or
liberty in their hands ? How many men in the
whole country would dare to take the risk ?
# * *
This is the plain justice of the matter. If the
law is to endure that a pauper prisoner by ask-
ing an unguarded question may open the door
bo evidence that will undo him, it is the bounden
duty of the judge to warn him what such a
question means, and give him an opportunity of
withdrawing it. Even that shouldn’t be enough
to satisfy the sentiment of fair play in a demo-
cratic community. It should not be left to the
judge to protect the prisoner’s interests. There
should be someone expressly appointed for the
purpose. There is a Crown Prosecutor to see
that the case against the accused is put as
strongly as possible, and the State ought
at the same time to provide a lawyer
to assist the accused and see that the
jury get a fair view of his side of the case.
The function of the Government in the ad-
ministration of justice is to give every man a
fair trial ; it has no more interest in the convic-
tion of the guilty than in the acquittal of the
innocent; and if it be necessary to pro-
vide a barrister at the expense of the
country to guard against the escape
of the guilty, it is a gross injustice that
a barrister is not on hand to help to keep the
innocent out of gaol.- This principle has been
already recognised in the case of prisoners
charged with capital offences, and there is no
possible ground on which objection could be
consistently made to its extension along the
whole scale of crimes and misdemeanours.
Keatinge, as events have proved, was on trial
for his life. He was 62 years of age, and just
after being sentenced to five years’ penal servi-
tude he died in his prison-cell.
That Recreation Room.
The Industrial School at Parramatta, where
young Sydney girls who get into the hands of
the police are sent to reform, is being run on
new lines. It has occurred to somebody to put
in practice the astounding theory that girls aged
from eight up to eighteen or so might occasion-
ally feel the want of some sort of relaxation,
and that they might stand a chance of develop-
ing into civilised womanhood if this want
were to some extent supplied. Accordingly
the well-behaved inmates are now allowed to
spend their evenings in the recreation-room,
with books, a piano, draught-boards, and other
unintoxicating instruments of dissipation, with
the extraordinary result that they are actually
growing in some measure contented and well-
behaved, and have left off prospecting with a
bread-knife for the matron’s scalp.
# # *
Now, Mrs. Grundy, what are you going to
do with those girls when in the course of time
your overflowing philanthropy prompts you to
take them into service in your Christian house-
hold? You will give them half-a-sovereign a
week, perhaps, and their keep, and in return
you will ask for nothing but gratitude and the
housework. But will you give them a chance
to spend their evenings in wholesome recrea-
tion ? Will you let them ask their friends in to
play draughts, or will they have any other
opportunity, except one afternoon a fortnight,
of enjoying congenial and innocent companion-
ship? You know, or you ought to know, Mrs.
Grundy, that human nature in the industrial
school and in the drawing-room is just on the
same pattern as human nature in the kitchen;
but will you treat those girls, when they become
your servants, as so much white woman like
yourself ?
# # *
No, Mrs. Grundy, you will not. They will
get up at 6 o’clock in the morning, and light
the lire, and bring you tea, and sweep the back
yard, and polish your husband’s boots, and
cook the meals, and wash and iron and do the
rooms, and scrub the floors, and keep the house
clean, and always be tidy to attend to the door,
and wait at table, and take the children out for
a walk in the afternoon, and run errands, and
do any other little jobs that want doing; and
so long as they are hardworking and respectful
and never answer back, perhaps you will not
throw it up in their teeth that they are only
charity-girls and ought to go down on their
knees to thank you for all your kindness. But
you will not permit them to indulge in ridicu-
lous ideas about recreation, and nonsense of that
sort. You don’t believe in letting maids think
they are “as good as their mistresses.” You
know how to keep your servants “in their pro-
per place.”
* * *
Exactly so. A..a if those girls come to get
utterly sick of the everlasting sound of the
treadmill, and to shudder at the vista of life-
long servitude ahead, and to think enviously of
the gaiety and freedom of girls they know who
didn’t happen to get reformed ; if they eftne to
feel, Mrs. Grundy, that a hard-working, vir-
tuous life isn’t worth the price they pay for it,
and go out to earn their living on the streets,
their blood wiU be on your head. Uo you
hear 2
PLAIN ENGLISH.
David Syme, Jingo.
The Melbourne Ace is suffering from the worst
attack of Jingoism in the head that has ever yet
descended upon that once Democratic paper. Its
latest idea is that Australia has got to raise an
army and send it along to India to assist in
rolling back the tide of Russian invasion, and
the reason of this extraordinary prospective
gyration is that should Russia conquer India
then Australia will be scattered and annexed
and broken up generally. In fact, the one hope
and opportunity of Australia is to send her
troops to whoop on the frozen peaks of Afghan-
istan, and unless they yell in the forefront of
the battle all is lost:—
Australia shares in the danger and is interested in meet-
ing it, whether from the Imperial or local point of view.
Even as an independent state Australia could not afford to
agree to an occupation of India by Russia ; in fact, our
danger would be all the greater. If the Russians reach
the sea-front the menace to Australia will be intolerable,
and Australia has its own interest in preventing this.
The defence of Australia begins on the hills outside
Herat.
There is one point here that the Age has over-
looked. If it is necessary for Australia to lend
a hand at shunting the Russians out of India —
should they arrive there —because their presence
some two or three thousand miles away would
be intolerable, why doesn’t Britain find it abso-
lutely necessary to shunt the French out of
France because their presence, some 20 miles
away from the English coast, must be a lot
more intolerable ? Why isn’t it essential that
she should bounce the Germans out of Prussia
for exactly the same cause, and give the
Spaniards notice to quit in Spain. And for the
same reason, why are not the Russians at St.
Petersburg as intolerable to England as the
Russians at Calcutta would be to Australia,
seeing that they are a great deal nearer,
and how is it that the mother country hasn’t
found it indispensable to rush them out of that
city ? Why doesn’t every nation find every
other adjacent nation an unbearable menace,
and why don’t they all find that they must
move each other further away, and if they did
make that discovery where would they move
each other to? If the defence of Australia
begins in Afghanistan it is quite obvious that
Australia must help to defend Afghanistan, but
the only way to do that would be to find out
where the defence of Afghanistan begins,
and if it starts as far away as the
Australian defences its outlying bulwarks must
either be at Moscow or Timbuctoo, and where
their defences begin no one knows. And sup-
posing there should be any mistake, and
America instead of Russia should prove to be
the coming enemy of this country, then the
defence of Australia clearly begins on the
Saskatchewan River or else in the interior of
Mexico. Further, how is it that there is nothing
conspicuously intolerable about the French in
New Caledonia, or the Germans in New Guinea,
or the Spaniards in the Carolines, as they arc
all much nearer than India, and are all strong
enough, if they chanced to boil over, to make
things hot for us ? And, lastly, where is
•Jingoism to end, for if the Russians rnvM be
driven out of Afghanistan for the safety of Aus-
tralia, then it is equally evident that they must
also be driven out of Turkestan for the safety
of Afghanistan, and out of some other place for
the safety of Turkestan, till at last we arrive at
a point where it becomes absolutely indispens-
able to drive everybody out of everywhere. In
fact, it might even be necessary to drive David
Syme out of the Age office, lest he should become
a menace to someone at the other end of the
street whose line of defence happened to begin
just in Mr. Same’s backyard.
A State Fraud.
Mr. Carruthers, the Nerv South Wales Minis-
ter for Public Instruction, personally bears the
reputation of a chivalrous man who would
neither devour widows’ houses nor cuckoo the
nests of orphans. But he is the head of a
department which systematically robs children
of their inheritance and females of their dues.
We are thinking of the case of the late Mrs.
Lockett, an old and faithful servant of
the State, and a highly-educated, refined, and
brainy woman. Mrs. Lockett was consecu-
tively schoolmistress in the State schools at
Forest Lodge, Camperdown, Newtown, Parra-
matta, William-street (Sydney), Hamilton, and
Scone. Her departmental record was of the
best, and she was known to the literary world
as a thoughtful contributor to the Nineteenth
Century and other English magazines. Her hus-
band seems to have been a bad lot —he deserted
her and committed bigamy, for which he received
a sentence of imprisonment in a neighbouring
province—and she was saddled with the onerous
task of bringing up her family by her own un-
aided efforts, a duty which she bravely and
conscientiously fulfilled. For years she had
contributed to the superanuation fund in her
department, and, shortly before her death, she
purposed resigning and proceeding to England,
where literary inducements had been held out
to her. While she was making the necessary
arrangements for resigning she died. Had she
been able to write out her resignation she
would have become entitled, out of the Superan-
nuation Fund, to a fair sum of money, which
she could have bequeathed to her children. But
she did not formally resign, and so the depart-
ment absorbed in itself the insurance fund that
she had for years paid out of her salary, and
refused to hand anything over to the
orphans, or for their benefit. A heartless
swindle of this sort by a private trustee
would meet with summary - punishment
at the hands of any Supreme Court judge
and jury, but it appears that the State may
be a licensed cheat and privileged marauder.
The heinousness of the fraud will be better
understood when it is remembered that all State
school teachers are compelled to pay a percentage
of their earnings into the Superannuation Fund;
in fact, the Department simply docks it out of
their salaries, without a “ By your leave.” If
a female teacher marries before a certain age,
the State robs her of her enforced past pay-
ments ; if she dies, the State usurps the birth-
right of her children. We ask Mr. < 'arruthers
how he likes this aspect of his official position
as cruel foster-parent of State babes and
sucklings and fraudulent trustee to honest
women ?

The “ Limits of Existence.”
A great, wild, vociferous Tory named Pincott
writes an article in the National Magazine,
in the course of which ho casts various new
lights upon the questions of labour and wages,
and draws attention to sundry facts about the
poor which no other commentator seems to
have noticed. The following observation is one
which only a man of “ the classes ” could have
written, and it deserves to be printed large on
its author’s tomb, and howled in his ear by a
bull-voiced ghoul through all the ages of
eternity ;
Whether pay be increased or the hours of labour
diminished the ultimate result will be the same, for the
poor will always, 6 y choice, live on the limits of existence.
Unless the British language has been mislaid,
this statement can only have one meaning—
that the poor are poor simply because they have
a unanimous desire to be poor, and that they
are violently opposed to any one who attempts
to encumber them with riches. Consequently
Mr. Pincott and all the rest of the tribe of
capitalists are doing them a noble service when
they totter along beneath the load of the big
bank-accounts which the masses shrink from
with horror, and drink the champagne and eat
the turkey which the toilers loathe and abomin <
ate ; and they are perpetrating an act of magni-
ficent self-sacrifice when they do no work,
because the British labourer wants all the work
himself, and the only thing he blasphemes at is
the pay. He lives on the “ limits of subsist-
ence ” by choice , and, as he chooses to live there,
it necessarily follows that he objects to any-
thing that threatens to alter his condition.
Therefore, the dockers’ strike and the great
railway convulsion and other troubles of the
same order were really attempts on the part of
the workman to get his wages lowered and
made more precarious—in fact, they were so
many efforts to get as near as possible to the
aforesaid “ limits ” that he is so fond of ; and
the difficulties in the coal-trade are an expres-
sion of the miner’s frenzied resolve to keep his
screw down or perish in the conflict. And in
like manner crime assumes anew aspect, and
burglary is a scheme by which the burglar
bursts open a house and feloniously deposits
property in it, because if he took property out
instead he would be manifesting a desire which
doesn’t exist to get away from the “ limits of ex-
istence and embezzlement is evidently a dodge
by which the underpaid clerk puts his own miser-
able salary in his employer’s cash-box ; and
garroting, we presume, is an evil-minded plan
by which a man chokes himself in the street,
and then forcibly presents his watch and his
loose cash to the unprotected passer-by. And
as to fraudulent insolvency complicated by
arson it must be in reality a method by which a
trader burns down his own premises, and after-
wards declines to accept the insurance, in order
to disguise the fact that he had bought all his
goods honestly, and paid for them twice over,
and then given them away to the poor, for if
man actually wants to live on the “limits of
existence” it is evident that he would run con-
siderable risks in the effort to get there, and
would make a serious row if anybody tried to
hinder him. And if these things are not so,
the only possible alternative is that the
Tory litterateur is either a drivelling hypo-
crite, or an incurable lunatic, or possibly
the two combined, and as for the editor who
publishes his maunderings the only expressions
which could adequately describe his mental
condition are of a kind that are utterly unfit
for publication.
The Bloated Dock-Labourer.
Leading Australian liars in a good way of busi-
ness will doubtless be interested in the following
statement which is going the rounds of the
press;—
Some of the dockers have been striking again in Lon-
don, and the statements have revealed a condition of
demoralisation among the men which is shocking and
alarming, skilled men refusing to work at 12s. 6d. a day,
and unskilled men demanding Is. 6d. to 2s. 6d. an hour,
and even at high wages taking thirteen days to unload a
ship which would have been emptied in three days at
Liverpool or New York.
It is a pity that the clumsy perjurer who invented
this statement forgot to mention that each one
of the starving, ragged, shoeless wretches who
are clamouring by hundreds for work in the
docks also insist on being made a duke and
having a landed estate thrown in. Also, he
overlooked the circumstance that the dockers
insist on working in evening dress, and that
many of them are on strike because the com-
panies refused this concession, and demanded
that they should wear frock coats and light
pants ; and he failed to allude to the fact that
most of them have been appointed Archbishop
of Canterbury, and that they have since struck
because they want to be John L. Sullivan at
the same time. Any intelligent and reliable
Ananias would have thrown in these statements
to lend an air of probability to his stupendous
lie, and he would hlso have explained
how his assertion squared with the well-known
fact that the dock-companies can get ten
times as many men as they want at sixpence an
hour, and that this miserable stipend is practi-
cally the sum total of the dockers’ ambition.
At the present moment, however, another huge
strike is in the air, for the companies are re-
solved to crush unionism, and take back the
miserable concession of one penny an hour which
they granted some time ago ; and the atrocious
perjury which we ! have quoted is one of the
preliminary moves to prevent their victims re-
ceiving any public sympathy or support. It is
true that the employers have nothing to gain
by reverting to the old order of things ; the
trifling improvement in the condition of the
men has produced such good results that no
amount of hard and violent lying could disguise
the fact that profits have increased and expenses
actually diminished under the new regime ; but
pride and mortification all demand that the men
should be “ put down ” —that capital should
“resent unwarrantable dictation”—and that
the great principle of “ freedom of contract ”
should be maintained in its entirety. There-
fore the great fight will, in all probability, have
to be fought over again, and as a preliminary
step an idiotic Tory press circulates the story
that the bloated labouring autocrat wants some-
thing like 255, a day, just as the same English
authority attempted to represent the Australian
strikers as demanding £4 or £5 a week, and as
the new season’s crop of lunatics is just in the
one statement will probably be credited in the
present, just as the other was in the past.
—.—
Catskin Trimmings.
The aldermanic flunkeys of Bendigo have just
carried out a sort of Lord Mayor’s Show of
their own. It was a piece of religious flummery
which preserved the worst features of British
beadledom. In England, an ancient custom
was for municipal authorities to “process” in
liveries on certain Sundays to the parish church
in order to demonstrate their continued loyalty
to “the Church by Law established.” But,
with the removal of religious tests, these useless
pageants have fallen into disuse even in
England. Here in Australia there is no State
Church, and civic dignities are open equally to
the adherents of all religious or the professors
of no religion at all. Yet the Bendigo aider-
men, on Sunday week, made a circus-parade to
the local Anglican church—the Mayor happen-
ing to be an Episcopalian. Had he been a J ew,
we suppose the aldermen would have tranquilly
trotted behind him to a synagogue on a Satur-
day. Had he been a Salvationist, they would
have rolled the old chariot along with knee-
drill, by drum and tambourine accompaniment,
Had he been an aggressive atheist, they would
have imported Jos, Symes and sung “ The
Celibate Parson and the Nut-brown Maid ”
as their opening anthem. These specu-
lations are not all far-fetched, in view
of what they really did do. First
walked Church of England parsons, in
their “ gowns next, the Mayor, in cocked
hat and official robes; Aldermen, in plug-lug
costumes ; Town Clerk, official ; City Solicitor,
summer clothes, belltopper and smile; Rate
Collector, ditto ; Cattle-market Inspector,
mufti; messenger, a youth in buttons. The
congregation mocked it at the church-door
with—“ Onward, Christian soldiers!” And
why all this '{ Because a more or less vulgar
Mayor wanted to show himself publicly in a
cocked hat and certain absurd robes trimmed
with catskin. We are glad to notice in Aus-
tralia generally an increasing disposition to
jeer at “robes” and “orders” ami loud uni-
forms, and wigs and ermine, and artillery
salutes, and all trash of . the kind, The more
“gorgeous” the robes and the greater the
pomp—so it is written in the history of the world
—the bigger the sham,
That the Builders and Contractors should hold
aloof from the aggressive Employers’ Union will
hardly surprise any person who has had any
dealings with these gentlemen. In no other
trade do such friendly feelings exist between
employer and employed, and a tone of perfect
equality prevails, from the hod-carrier, to the
boss himself. In fact, it is not unusual for the
men to address the master by his Christian
name. The reason is not hard to discover : the
successful builder is a practical tradesman, and
must have undergone long pupilage at real
“hard graft;” lie therefore is fully aware of
the difficulties under which wages are earned,
and can sympathise with the man who considers
his remuneration inadequate. Unfortunately,
the prices obtainable do not permit of high
wage-giving, as the sweaters of the building-
trade are the architects and proprietors. It is
impossible for anyone of the masher species to
succeed at contracting; it is the one line of
business in which mere theory is useless,
Accordingly, where no class prejudices exas-
perate the workers at every turn, and where
respect is offered and given on all sides, and
where half of the employed have prospects of
ultimately becoming employers themselves, the
seething hostilities which characterise other
trades are conspicuous by their absence.
Judge Moleswokth, charging a Melbournt
jury in the Stella Marks murder case, took a
queer view of the “ temporary insanity ” ques-
tion. “If the prisoner had sufficient reason to
know he was doing an act that was wrong, he
is responsible for his crime. If he had not
sufficient reason to know he was doing an act
that was wrong he is entitled to be acquitted on
the ground of insanity,” said His Honor. This
Cockney definition of madness in its bearing
upon murder is so uncommonly narrow that
probably no murder has ever come within it.
Undoubted lunatics, after committing a murder,
have shown the greatest cunning in endeavour-
ing to divert suspicion from themselves. The
common or garden kind of insanity is supposed
to be that condition of mind which incites a
person to do something senseless, foolish, rash,
or wrong, whilst rendering the said person in-
capable of resisting any strong impulse. One
would like to have Coroner Youl’s opinion on
the Moleswokth doctrine. The man who
writes, “I am going to end my troubles. God
forgive me !” and then jumps into the Yarra, is
evidently aware that he is doing something
which the Church and State call wicked. But
a coroner—usually the best of public authori-
ties on temporary insanity—advises his jury
that “ deceased committed suicide by drowning,
whilst of unsound mind.” And the world says
“ hear, hear.” This doesn’t prove that the
slayer of giddy Miss Marks was mad when
•overcome by his jealous impulse, but it seems
to negative the proposition that a man must
necessarily bo sane when doing something
which he knows to be an offence against the
law, or even against his God. It is worth
noticing, too, that the Moleswokth doctrine,
if accepted, would dispose of kleptomania in
one act, seeing that sufferers from this strange
complaint always recognise their own responsi-
bility by secreting stolen goods underneath
their waistcoats or inside their bustles.

We remember the time, a few years ago,
when “ persons of gentler type ” did “ utterly
abhor ” the Age on account of the fearless, not
to say reckless, manner in which that paper
“ridiculed matters of creed,” shattered the
idols of sectarianism, and “ shocked ” the sen-
sibilities of public men who could not afford to
have their private life unveiled to the guffawing
gaze of the intensely-interested public. It is a
curious coincidence that in those days, of which
the Age now visibly repents, that newspaper
had a mighty and vital influence over the people
of Victoria. The Age was not wont to sack
caramels and mumble with a lisp in those lurid
Berry times when it chronicled paians of jubi-
lant prophecy about ‘ 1 broken heads and flaming
houses,” petted “ Berry, the buster-up,” eulo-
gised Woods, the Wat-Tyler of the time,
haloed Lalor, the past-rebel, and pitchforked
into political life O’Loghlen, the first man to
publicly suggest “ cutting the painter,” &c., &c,
But David Bvme has changed all that. He has
grown wealthy on the pence of the million, and
his riches have begotten new sensibilities “ of
gentler type.” With its new-born reverence
for credal fetishes the Age thus become
“respectable,” and has consequently lost all
but its purely commercial influence with the
masses. Its ‘ 1 vulgarity of mind and educa-
tion ” are carefully covered with the fig-leaves
of conscious hypocrisy,
In one of the Sydney Police Courts lately , '*
man named Livermore was charged before
Stipendiary Abbott (now on sick-leave) with
indecent exposure. The S.M. said he regretted
much that he was unable to order a Hogging, as
he happened to be sitting alone, but, added he
significantly, “I will send you for trial where
you will be liable to 14 years’ penal servitude.”
At the trial in the superior court the jury,
after being locked up all night, disagreed, and
w'ere discharged. The Attorney-General has
since—and this is where the satire on Mr.
Stipendiary Abbott and tire brutal code of laws
which he and his brethren dispense comes in—
refused to go any further with the case, and
Livermore has been set at liberty ! Thus the
crime which Mr. Abbott was so sure had been
committed, and over which he bewailed his
inability to summarily order a flogging, has, in
the opinion of a jury, not been proved ; nor in
the deliberate and dispassionate opinion of
Attorney-General Simpson (who, considering
that he had the murderess, Louisa Collins,
tried three times and eventually convicted, can-
not be accused of chicken-heartedness in such
matters) is there evidence before the Crown to
justify the man being tried again ? So much
for hasty floggings at the mere behest of a
couple of beaks. If you give a pair of mere
magistrates power to flog a man, why not give
them power to hang him?
A duty cast on every man by law is mat of
supporting his wife and family. To neglect
this duty is an offence for which a man may be,
and occasionally is, sent to gaol. He may also
be, and frequently is, sent to gaol for other
offences. Certain it is that whatever he goes
therefor, so long as he is there he can’t support
his innocent family, and unless they can find
some other means of subsistence they must
starve. Will someone please show some valid
reason why the Government—the administrator
of the law, the power that has taken away their
breadwinner—should not support them till it
gives him a chance of doing so himself?
Why should it not undertake the duty the law
says he must discharge, but which it prevents
him from discharging ? A citizen is temporarily
withdrawn from circulation because his fellow’-
citizeus in their collective capacity think that
course is conducive to their welfare, and there-
upon a number of perfectly innocent people are
driven to look for bread in the poorhouse and on
the streets. What has an enlightened demo-
cratic humanity to say to this? “For God’s
sake consider my poor wife and children!”
groans the convicted man in the dock. ‘‘l am
sony for them,” returns the judge, “but it is
you who should have considered them.” When
woman gets a vote will she be more just than
man has been to the wives and children of men
sent to gaol in the public interest?

Church of England, and yet the other five-sixths
of the people are robbed under the tithe-system to
support a church in which they don’t believe. The
cunning of the Anglican prelates keeps the
English Church income from being known It is
supposed to be about six millions annually.

810 Maoquafie-street North, Sydney, March 4, 1891.
My dear Moorabinda,—
A dense crowd without the pale and a chosen few
inside on the platform were the only outward and visible
signs at Redfern on 25th ultimo that heralded the approach
of Lady Jersey. Not a twig was put up to decorate the ugly
station, nor was a yard of the good old crimson drugget
to be seen, and if it hadn’t been for the 180 Fort-street
children in white frocks and blue sashes, all carrying
flowers which they scattered before the Countess, the
arrival would have been a woefully colourless one.
Mayor Burdekin, in a festive w r hite carnation button-
holder, brought Miss Burdekin in figured blue cambric
with dark-blue velvet trimmings; and another well-
known gentleman had his wife with him, but otherwise the
committee of welcome—including the inevitable Dan, Mr,
E, T. Cracknel!, Mr. Unmaok (Postmaster-General of
Queensland), who is here for the conference, and
Lieutenant-Colonel Macdonald (Brisbane) —wore a singu-
larly “ smoke-night ” air. After leaving the train Lady
Jersey was presented by Miss Lily Bridges w ith a well-
put-up bouquet of amaryllis, tiger lilies, and crimson-and-
saffron roses tied with the Jersey colours of blue
and yellow; the “little Jerseys” also received
bouquets, all of which Were laid on by Minister
Carruthers. Of course, an infant murmured a verbal
(for this relief much thanks) address of the usual type,
and in her reply Lady Jersey showed that even if her
voice is more pipey than resonant, she has mastered the
beautiful art of cutting it short.
The new social beacon has popularity written all over
her. She is a medium-sized woman with a kind, clever
face beautifully devoid of cosmetics, dark-brown hair
simply drawn up in a knot on the top of her head, snow-
white teeth and brown eyes. She looks thirty-three or
four, though in reality she is among the forties, and her
figure is slim and erect. She wore, on arriving, a copper*
coloured silk gown covered with black-spotted net and
la«e, with panels on kite right side of copper-coloured rep
ribbon with loops of the ribbon across fastened with a
rosette, a black jet belt round- the waist, and a black jet
collar fastened with a diamond star brooch ; small black
lace and jet bonnet with black strings and cluster of
honeysuckle on the crown, copper-silk parasol veiled in
black chiffon, a black tulle half veil, tern kid gloves and
gold chain bangle- Her daughters wore workmanlike
frocks of cream serge made with straight skirts feather-
stitched round the hem with white silk and long double-
breasted jackets with Capuchin hoods, sailor hats, with a
band of white ribbon and cream gossariier veils. They
are two pale fair girls wearing their hair flowing and
brushed bock from their foreheads-.
Unlike Lady Carington, the Countess of Jersey doesn’t
wear a bang but combs her hair off like hubby does, and
so the frizzly-poodle female whose eyebrows no human
being ever sees will be hopelessly left. What a revelation
there will be if fringes become bad form in Sydney and
the Jersey poll catches on ! For one’s forehead seems to
gather to itself all the depressing wrinkles in one’s
system, and the belle who looks 19 with her hair curled
round her temples turns out a bald 40when she wears her
cranial locks Ala cock’s-comb. Yet still there is balm' in
Gilead for those women who suffer from high foreheads
and must cover them to keep them from looking like a.
death’s head on a toasting fork. Whatever may be the
anti-fringe fad in Sydney, the Princess of Wales, the-
“ great I am” of what is correct to Wear, still clings to-
iler curly front.
There must be some mystic vice-regal meaning in blue
neckties. Gov. Jersey has taken to wearing one which
leaves Carington’s a faded failure, and as it doesn’t har-
monise with the rich tints of his back hair why does he
wear it ? Perhaps as a sort of polite blue ribbon to show
that “ it ” really is caused by indigestion.
A good idea for women whose hair is demoralised by the
damp night air, and who don’t wish to go forth to even-
ing shivoos wrapped up to their eyes like mummies, is to-
fix a tulle or net fall—pale-pink is the least noticeable and
most becoming shade—over the face and hair ere putting
on the “fascinator.” This keeps the pericranial curls in
decent condition until you “get there’’—besides which
the—er —powder doesn’t blow off before the fun begins.
There is much argument as to the form of local reception
to be given to Lady Jersey, who is to visit the City of
Asylums on 19th inst. Some of the aldermen are for an
extended garden-party, others for a reception with re-
freshment, but so far nothing definitely has been arranged'
barring the date.
There is a skewbald time ahead for those foolish virgins;
and others who bleached their non-committal tresses a
light tan-colour and then, having coquetted with peroxide-
of hydrogen, came forth silkworra-hued, since yellow
frizzes are quite out of fashion and blue-black hair is the-
only wear. What this will lead to is easily prophesied.
In my mind's eye, Horatio, I can see the streaky coils;
where the gold-wash still gleams through the stove-polish
and the peroxide and the black lead refuse to amalgamate..
The colour at first would be a rusty tint like a black-
poodle in need of a bath, but probably if the patient
perseveres and lays it on thick and doesn’t mind her hair
coming out in fistfuls she may eventually turn out bakl-
headed or raven-hued—that is it she doesn’t go mad in
the intcriyi from dye on the brain. No naturally fair-
haired woman will take any notice of the brunette craze
or dip her waving “glory” in the inkstand. For dark
hair will never enjoy more than a brief run; that it does
not light up well under the electric light being the great
reason.
One of woman’s weirdest features is the style in which
she invariably glares down the other party, even though
t he hapless wight (who is generally a man) is the only one
really justified in showing tantrums. If you ride in any
public vehicle at the hour when weary masculinity is
being bumped or rattled home to its evening meal, en-
wrapped (the man, not the meal) in the damp sheets of
of the noozepaper, you can’t but be struck by the number
of conscienceless women who devour his columns with a
palpable side-eye; and when some Brute, churlishly
objecting to share his penn’orth with an utter stranger,
pointedly folds his paper up so that he alone can have the
benefit of it, the withering and indignant glare of his
oppressor is hot enough to shrivel up a regiment'.
It really seems as if the average she can’t keep her eyes
off itinerant literature. To produce and read a letter
(especially if it is in a mannish fist) in the presence of
women is equal to waving a red rag at a bull. Should
you by chance look up from the thrilling page, you will
see half the women with their eyes on their cheeks to find
out what it is all about, while those out of range have you
fixed with a merciless stare as if they would read your
soul from the trimming on your hat. A spirit of rivalry,
too, is often engendered in the feminine bosom at the
appearance of an envelope. Somebody once told me that
she began to read a letter in an omnibus half-full of
darlings with their hands in their laps. In the twinkling
of a tack each woman got a crumpled-up sheet out of her
pocket and became breathlessly absorbed in its well-worn
contents.
The awesome naivetd of some women is a fearful and
wonderful thing. I was on the railway recently, with a
mixed company on board, and behind me sat two little
dears, whose free and gladsome cackle was audible all
over the train. Said one: “ And so Frank and I went
and had dinner, but I didn’t eat anything.” “Win - ,
weren’t you hungry?” queried her companion. “Dread-
fully, simply starving, but I just wouldn’t let Frank see
me eat. Didn’t I have a good meal when I got home
though.”
Somebody recently waved a frantic pen on the
puzzling subject of Woman's Dress—it was only too
obviously a he—while determining that the broad lines of
female dress were shaped by the “masculine element,”
said darkly “ The small modifications made by women in
the fashion prescribed for them arise solely,” &c., &c.
But “modification ” is an ill-fitting term to aim at Women
and clothes, seeing that exaggeration is the prevailing-
feminine weakness. For instance, crinolettes, bashfully
introduced by Worth, led to balloon-like crinolines ; from
the diminutive bag of cotton wadding was evolved the
gigantic improver which flourished like a green bay-tree
for more than one season. Everybody remembers how,
when high hats came in, women never rested till they
worked them up to pyramids, and to-day the fashionable
frockist is often told to cut the gored skirt a little scanter
than the rest, and to ridge the gigot sleeves quite up to
the car. In fact, women who “dress” never “modify”
if they can possibly help it. They leave all that to the
twenty-four-inch-waisted hygienic creature who is always
Careful to look and see if her new hat has ventilation-
holes.
Apropos to gored skirts, tile girl of the period is begin-
ning to enter more deeply into her brother’s feelings
when she sees the knees of his continuations drooping
baggily, for the gored skirt does precisely the same
thing. There was a singularly tight one oil the Block the
other day with two distinct bags where the wearer's knees
fitted in when she walked, and the effect was very funny.
If gored skirts remain in much longer a stretcher will
have to be included in one’s dressing-ease.
Poor but popular young men who mourn the nap as
it flies from the brims of their hats through repeated
salutations to the sex will be glad to know that the latest
Paris way of acknowledging a woman is to raise the hand
to the forehead in the military manner, or else “ to press
the palm against the heart, gently stooping meanwhile,’
This last recipe must look a good deal like cramps until
you get used to it, hut it will give men’s hat-brims a
chance.
In Bland Holt's thrill, “ Master and Man,” at Sydney
Royal, as the action oscillates between a ’umble ’ome and
a boiler-factory the tear-soaked heroine (Kate Bishop) is
necessarily persecuted in mufti, and the sprightly foil
(Mrs. Bland Holt) wears her dimpled elbows ready for
action most of the time. Kate Bishop has a well-cut
sage-green dress and flat leghorn hat trimmed with blow
aways in the first act, and pretty Mrs. Bland Holt is
Dresden Chinaish in a cream skirt with a long-tailed top
flowered in crimson pompadour, but the rest of the Rock-
ing is outer darkness. “ Master and Man ”is beautifully
staged, from the waving corn of the first act to the lurid
realism of the “ works,” and every woman in the audience
heaves a sigh of content when the cradle, in the second
act, delivers up a real live baby instead of the painfully
wooden monstrosity which usually hangs over the
heroine’s shoulder like a miniature Aunt Sally. There
was a full circle on the first night.
“ Arrah-na-Pogue,” at Her Majesty’s, is a tearful
theme, ending up happily on the roof, and in it Edith
Blande wears her own hair and a beautiful old-time dinner
dress of green brocade bodice and train, edged with gold
galon, the jupe and front of gold tissue, satin, and sage
green passementerie; the sleeves of gold tissue showing
the arm. Sweeney—l just love Sweeney’s Colonel
O’Grady—is nice enough to paint in a scarlet coat, a
white wig and winning little beauty-spots round his rose-
bud mouth; and Greenaway, in a periwinkle-shaped
beaver, shins up the ladder leading to the garret in a way
that only wants a hod to make it p<t i feet. The scenery
all through is beautiful, especially the moonlight in the
last act, and the cheers which greeted the appearance of
the geegee in the jaunting-car will probably induce that
stage-stricken animal to essay Hamlet on his hind legs.
The story goes that a certain NCS.W. Minister doesn’t
go through his office work quite so carefully as did his
predecessor, and in consequence of this rather an awkward
mistake happened recently. It seems that when an M.P.
goes below or above, as the case may be, the matter is at
once officially reported to the Ministers (though I’m sure
I don’t know why they should be informed since they
aren’t “in the undertaking,”)and accordingly when a
member died a little while ago they sent our hero up a
communication to that effect. But it came back in a twink-
ling with the Minister’s ear-mark “Approved” scrawled
across it. Which reminds one of that old story about the
N.S.W. Supreme Court clerk, who was notorious for sign,
ing almost anything put before him, and whom some
sportive subordinates once induced to put his name to
his own death-warrant.
Yours affectionately,
SAPPHO SMITH.

A very dressy wedding was that of Miss Alize Fitz-
maurice, daughter of Mr. G. R. Fitzmaurice, “ Verncr,’'
Croydon, Sydney, to Mr. Hugh Dixon, of “ Abergeldie,”
Summer Hill, on 25th ult., at St. James’, Croydon, which
was a bower of white blossoms. The bride wore a white
gown of faille Framjaise over a jupe of brocaded satin
designed in lilies of the valley, the front of the bodice of
crepe de chine with a sash of crepe de chine on the left
side, high sleeves and Medici collar of brocade, wreath of
orange-blossoms and heather, and exquisite white bou-
quet ; her four bridesmaids (Miss Fitzmaurice, Miss Dixon,
Miss Helen Dixon and Miss Kelynack) wearing ivory
canvas cloth striped with silk, with puffed sleeves relieved
with deep frills of heliotrope ribbons from the left
shoulder; coarse straw hats trimmed with mauve and
white chiffon ; white parasols with heliotrope bows and
bouquets tied to the handles. Breakfast was held at
“Abergeldie,” after which Mr. and Mrs. Hugh Dixon left
for their honeymoon in Melbourne, the bride wearing
green cashmere with braces and cuffs of favourite passe-
menterie, black lace hat with crown of pleated dark-green
velvet and black feathers.

Mr. Geoffrey Eagar’s retirement from the
N.S.W. Under-Secretaryship of Finance and
Trade has not occasioned universal regret in the
departments over which he was for so long the
practical autocrat. In the Treasury it has been
hailed with relief. A similar feeling pervades
the Custom House and the Government Printing-
Office. When Tom Richards was chief of the
latter department there was continual friction
between the two men. This originated when Mr.
Eagar was Colonial Treasurer. One of Eagar’s
pet projects was to break up the Government
Printing Office on the alleged ground that the
work could be done more cheaply and efficiently
by private printing-firms, and it is said to have
been in anticipation of this that one of the largest
Sydney printing-firms imported its plant. Sir
Henry Parkes (then plain Mister) defeated the
project in the Cabinet of which Sir James Martin
(also then plain Mister) was Premier. While
Treasurer, Eagar also came into collision with the
late W. A. .Duncan. As Treasurer, Eagar was
Duncan’s official superior. The Collector of Cus-
toms, an unbending Scotchman, declined to per-
form a certain action which the Treasurer forced
upon him, and in doing so used a certain inde-
pendence of language which Eagar and Martin
(over whom the former seemed to have an utterly
unaccountable influence) construed into insubor-
dination. In spite of the pleadings of Parkes, the
Colonial Secretary, the Cabinet dismissed Duncan
and an officer upon whom Eagar had always
looked with favour was appointed in his place.
Governor Young protested, and delayed his assent
so long as possible, but the tenacious Martin ulti-
mately carried the day. The victory cost him and
the Cabinet dearly. Parkes promptly resigned,
the Ministry was defeated, and Sir John Robert-
son returned to office. Duncan met Robertson in
the street. “ Don’t say a single word now,” said
the Watson’s Bay veteran, “we’ll have you back
before you can say Jack Robertson ! ” And with
this very fair witticism he passed on to the Cabinet
meeting which re-instated Duncan ten minutes
after it met. This catastrophe to the Martin
Government dismissed Eagar from public life, but
the back door of the Treasury was open and
through this the wily ex-Minister came as a
subordinate, exercising his peculiar influence over
successive Treasurers, even “Billy” M‘Millan
falling under his subtle sway. With Eagar as
Under-Secretary and Duncan as Collector, and
the memory of their now historic quarrel abiding
with them, the relations of the two departments
were, to say the least, strained, until ’death re-
moved Duncan. It was characteristic of Eagar
that he had a long and vindictive official memory,
and when the almost unlimited departmental in-
fluence possessed by him over the three Treasury
departments is remembered it can easily be under-
stood what that meant. Mr. Eagar is a native-
born Australian, and was once popularly known
as “Betsy.”

So gently smile and press the button,
And order up a feed of meat.

Bland Holt opened his Sydney Royal sea-
son with “ Master and Man ” to a tremendous
house and a warm reception from all hands. This
play seems to have started out with a desire to
achieve originality ; but, somehow, except in one
or two situations, its leaves have been gathered
beneath the spreading chestnuts whence they
fluttered down, sere and yellow, many autumns
ago. Most of its comedy, too, belongs to the
bygone bladder school that we fondly dreamed
was relegated to the limbo of the nigger farce and
the harlequinade. The acting is all right—Arthur
Lawrence can’t have much room left for marma-
lade on his little platter, for the press everywhere
has eulogised his Humpy Logan, but we must,
at the risk of a surfeit, make him swallow our
spoonful. His Humpy Logan is a closely-studied,
intelligently-handled delineation of the villain,
known only to the stage, who never relaxes his
efforts to humiliate and ruin the woman he loves
because she loves someone else. After his force-
ful acting in the furnace scene, the actor’s best
point was made in the first act when he strongly
emphasised the lines in which Humpy’s better
man shows itself ; lines made necessary by his
sudden change of front in the last act. In Tom
Honeywood, Bland Holt is fitted with one of
these perky, smart characters he handles so easily
and has the house with him all the time. ’Gus
Glover is powerful, but his dialect is not always
correct; his denunciations have the true ring,
but he pitches his lines just a little too
much to the gallery; his stuff doesn’t want
that, and would get there without. Walter
Howe is very annoying in the first act where
he walks about with an aint-I-pretty look,
but prison garb quenches his jauntiness and he is
latterly less offensive. Albert Norman as Carlton,
the polished scoundrel, looks the part and speaks
his lines well, and the St. Jones of H. R. Roberts
would be tolerable if the buffoonery were weeded
out, and if some of his business had been less pal-
pably pitch-forked into the show. Kate Bishop
and her baby deserve favourable mention ; Mrs.
Holt is charming and vivacious; May Vivian and
Flora Anstead are equal to their small oppor-
tunities, but Beelzebub was the hero of the even-
ing. George Gordon deservedly took a big call
for his foundry-scene, indeed the scenery through*
out is very fine.
* * »
At Sydney Garrick “II Trovatore ” ran
for three nights to good business. Its most
remarkable feature was the sweet vocalisation
and bright acting of Nina Osborne in Inez, a very
small part. Now “ The Bohemian Girl ” fills the
bill fully. Viewed after a lapse of time, the story
seems incoherent and not particularly interesting.
One would also like to know how it is that
while Arline grows up from babyhood to a
marriageable age none of the other people, bar
the Count, add a wrinkle or turn a hair.
Annis Montague, now in very good voice,
sings the music charmingly, and cavorts round
as giddily as adipose tissue will allow.
Charles Turner has a lovely cold, a relation of
Warwick Gainor’s, and he palpably saves himself
nightly for “The Fair Land of Poland,” which
got a triple encore on the night of our visit.
Gainor is so hoarse that it is wonderful how he
drags through. Cicely Staunton’s Gipsy Queen
is the best-acted part in the cast, and the Devils-
hoof of Fred. England is much better than we
anticipated. “Mignon,” by Arabroise Thomas,
goes up on Saturday night. As previously an-
nounced, the Turners will be followed at this
house by Janet Achurch, who will be succeeded
by J. L. Sheridan, who will be ousted, it is said,
by Jennie Lee.

It is said that Grattan Riggs can imitate
four or five Irish dialects perfectly. George Rig-
nold (now restored to health) apparently knows
forty or fifty, but can’t make up his mind which
would go best with his hand-knit hose, so his
patter, like Sydney pavements, is a bit patchy.
All round, the performance is very much im-
proved, however, especially in the case of Harry
Jewett. “My Jack ”is said to be still in rehear-
sal, but “George” has at least five or six other
trifles in hand, and if he were to spring “Struck
Oil” or “The Messiah,” with himself in the
name-part, on the public no one would be sur-
prised. Now we hear that the Irish season pre-
ceding Easter will be occupied with something to
suit Maggie Moore. By the way, the Newsboys’
Brigade will be matineed at Her Majesty’s on
Wednesday, 11th March, under the patronage of
the Jerseys.

There is little to be said about “The Jilt,”
at Sydney Criterion, except that business has
been good, and that the withdrawal of Arthur
Lawrence gave George L. Gordon, out of the bill
for some time, a chance to appear as Daisy. The
dailies speak well of his performance; we didn’t
see it. “School ”is announced for Tuesday night,
and the reappearance of George Anson as Beau
Farintosh. The Beau isn’t one of his best.
“ Caste,” “ Ours,” “ The Squire,” are all under-
fined for production, it is said, but the next big
thing will be Tom Taylor’s “New Men and Old
Acres,” for which Churchward has come over to
paint the scenery.
At Grattan Riggs’ farewell appearance and
benefit, on Friday night last, “ The Yankee
Farmer,” a good old homespun story, was played,
in which the beneficiary’s fine New England dialect
surprised those who had only seen him in Irish
character, and J. P. West played an amorous
dotard with acceptation. “ Handy Andy ” fol-
lowed. It’s an age since we saw it last—it may
'be a chestnut, but there’s fun in it yet. Its most
noticeable feature was the drunken-scene between
Andy and the Murphy of Charles Seagrave. That
alone was worth the entrance-fee. The genial
and unassuming Riggs has sailed for Melbourne.
* * *
The boxing match at Coogee Aquarium
between Ryan and Williams was won by the
former in the fourteenth round of a Y.M.O.A.
encounter. Williams, the Bathurst heavy-paper-
weight, is a fat, flabby and jumbovine slugger
who prefers ha’pence to licks, and would sooner
sit down quickly than be sent to grass. Now 7 ,
his paper-made reputation is burst like a wind-
filled bun-bag, but he should have a fine future
before him as a policeman. Ryan is a finely-built
man, who has a lot to learn and time to learn it in.
* * * *
At Sydney Gaiety, the Rickards matinee
was a bumper and the benefit at night filled the
building to boiling-over. Many bouquets were
sent across the footlights and Mrs. Rickards re-
ceived a very large and lovely wreath as a token
of goodwill from Mr. and Mrs. Davys, members
of the company. Exhausted by singing encore
songs at the close, “ ’Arry,” announced that he had
accepted a lump sum to extend the season for six
nights with a new bill. So far business has been
good.

The last days of Dr. Carver at Moore Park
are announced. On Saturday there will be a
change of programme, and the eminent shootist
will do some fancy work in the way of ball-break-
ing against time. Prices have been reduced
slightly and, barring wet days, the attendance is
good.

At Bondi Aquarium last Saturday a per-
fect gale prevented Fernandez from filling the
balloon ; consequently, he couldn’t go up. This is
his first failure, and he will most likely make
another attempt next Saturday.
