* * * *
The Dual Swimming Club, which meets at
Pitt-street Natatorium on Wednesday evenings,
is five weeks old and has already a membership of
132. One side of the bath, to the water’s edge, is
screened by canvas beneath which ladies, robed
in the club’s uniform, a loose costume of navy-blue
sergerette, may drop into the water unobserved.
Initial shyness and an innate repugnance to wet
the back hair overcome, however, they wade in
with as much daring and abandon as the boldest
among the rougher sex. Indeed, at the deep end
ladies may be seen jumping off the spring board
and sliding down the shoot in the most matter-
of-fact way. The shallow end, however, provides
the best sport. There husbands may be seen
coaching their wives and little ones, some of
whom look scarcely old enough to be trusted
alone on the land, paddle about in all directions.
The bath can be maintained at an even tempera-
ture, right through the year, by the application of
steam from below, and is provided with hot and
cold showers. The hotel adjoining is clean, com-
fortable and well-appointed, and likely to become
popular.
* * * *
Arthur Gordon’s Sunday afternoon and
evening water concerts have caught on. Last
Sunday night the Alathea was filled from stem to
stern, though not uncomfortably. After a run
up the Lane Cove River, where the concert was
given, she arrived at the Quay at 10 o’clock.
Several popular vocalists performed, and Harry
Rickards, having sung four songs, was not
allowed to sit down until he had promised to
“oblige again” next Sunday evening. Charlie
Faning will be “thar” in the afternoon, when
Tenor Cadzow will make a dulcet noise.
The omission of the little word “not”
from last notice of “The Middleman,” at Mel-
bourne Royal, made it appear that the house was
filled everywhere save in the dress-circle on
opening night. Matters were just otherwise,
however,_ and so remained until Saturday, when
business in the upper and lower regions indicated
that the merits of J ones’ strong play were begin-
ning to be noised abroad.
* * #
No one who was present on the first night
imagined that Cyrus Blenkarn would so soon be
confronted by dusty seats and benches, where the
casual spider found himself again at liberty to
renew the web that had been religiously swept
away by the brush of skirts and coat-tails during
pantomime season. But evidently the public
are slow to believe in drama at the Royal
after their bitter experience of the last
few months. Poor attendances have ap-
peared to depress Cartwright and weaken his
performance to some extent, nevertheless “ The
Middleman ” is respectfully spoken of by those
who saw it under the gloomiest of conditions.
Sass is perhaps too young and sprightly-mannered
for the pompous manufacturer, nor does he make
any serious attempt to humbug his family or him-
self. One can easily picture the character being
more effectively played on entirely different lines,
yet the present Joseph Chandler is consistent
enough all through, notwithstanding his Sassiness.
On the other hand, there is no sort of sense about
the conduct of Eille Norwood, who finally returns
from a two years’ tour of Europe wearing the same
suit of clothes that he started off in. This is
monstrous. Blenkarn might wear one set of
garments all that time, because he is a towzled
dreamer. But Captain Chandler, being merely a
creature of his tailor, couldn’t possibly retain the
affections of poor Mary if he were to cease
appearing in a nice new rig such as won her
heart in the first instance. No matter. “The
Middleman ” is about to retire in disgust, and on
Saturday (7th) the Cartwright-Nethersole com-
pany appear in “The Fortune of War,” for the
first time on any stage. This may, or may not,
be as sterling a drama as the other. Anyway,
it will have the assistance of 100 stage sojers and
a deal of gunpowder explosion. There is also
reason to hope that “ Rule Britannia ” will crop
up during the disturbance.

an unaesthetic lock down over his godlike brow
just as though he were coming round from the
butcher for orders. Ryley will not be doing his
duty to sassiety until he sleeps with that lump of
hair in papers, and comes out at night in a curly
halo.

“Pepifea” is to be performed with many
variations from the original Melbourne cast,
which included Woodfield, Marnock, Nellie
Stewart, and Fanny Liddiard. Elton will get
there again, though, as our dearest and best
Pataquet; and this reminds one that Elton’s days
in Australia are numbered, and his farewell benefit
a function of the near future.
* * * *
“ The Miner’s Right ” goes along steadily
at the Alexandra, and even though Dampier does
strike more gold on the stage than in the treasury
he has no particular cause for grumbling, all
things considered. The gallery boys support him
through thick and thin. fc When somebody is
winding him up from his alluvial claim in a
bucket you can hear the youngsters scream,
“Look out for Dampy!” And when Dampy is
brought to the surface the welkin rings itself
hoarse.
« « « *
“ Doctor Bill ” is coming to a hilarious
end at the Bijou, and B. & B. will know him no
more after Friday, the W. & G. firm having made
arrangements to run Myra Kemble and her show
as far as New Zealand, with a fresh troupe of
kangaroo-dancers, and different doorkeys to those
now in vogue. The A church season opens on
Saturday with “Devil Caresfoot,” as already
notified. Herbert Flemming (who has graciously
consented to delay his departure for London, vide
advt.) takes a hand, and George Leitch, the his-
torical Insect, will likewise come in at some stage
of the proceedings.
* * * *
At the Opera House they promise a three-
act comedy called “Kindred Souls,” in place of
“Skipped.” This mixed entertainment has
whirled around for four weeks, and the world
wondered when it intended to stop. That a col-
lection of songs, gags, incidental yarns, and baby-
chases could revolve round an oyster-stall for a
whole month is only to be accounted for by the
ability of Snazelle, Gourlay and Co. to do nothing
in particular, but do it very well. Some fresh
people are to be added for “Kindred Souls,” which
was played at Sydney Criterion some few years
ago.
* * * *
A litter of performing pigs and a new
first part drew a screaming crowd to the Gaiety
on Saturday night. The influence of racing times
was perceptible, too, at the little Vic., the Wax-
works, Cyclorama, and a few other places, whilst
several backers of Bungebah celebrated the occa-
sion by dropping sixpence down a phonograph-
slot and getting some sort of noise for their
money. The present writer had a sixpenn’orth at
one of these machines when it wasn’t turned on
properly or something, and the result was a muffled
buzzing which sounded like a cross between Glad-
stone’s speech and a comic song, but was subse-
quently represented to have been a flageolet solo
from America. As a sound emanating from a
small box it was not lacking in interest. As a
musical performance it was demnable.

Williamson and Garner have taken a
“ Co.” into the business.
* » *
E. A. Stacey's “Hans the Boatman”
company is playing at the Adelaide Royal to good
houses, the simple story of the picturesque Swiss
boy having apparently lost none of its interest
since Charlie Arnold first appeared in that
character. Stacey has had the misfortune to lose
his dog, an important character in this little
comedy, but has substituted a prize-winning St.
Bernard, who introduced so much unrehearsed
business into his part during the week that his
histrionic success is somewhat doubtful. Stacey,
as Hans, and Miss Madge Grey, as Effie, have
both established themselves as favourites, and the
rest of the company has been up to the average.
Myra Kemble, supported by “Teddy” Royce
and other good people, opens there at Easter with
“ Dr. Bill.” A Panorama of Australia is being
exhibited at Garner’s Rooms, Adelaide, to fair
business. Mr. Fred Neebe makes an efficient
lecturer, and as the majority of the pictures are
well painted, the show is likely to catch on.
At Poverty Point.
Somebody remarked the other day, in print, how extra-
ordinary it was that three or four members of the amateur
dramatic club, to which Labertouohe, of Sydney,, be-
longed years ago, should have come to unhappy ends.
The remark evinces a sad lack of worldly knowledge and
reflection. To the man with a long memory and a soft
heart who looks back on the roll of those who during a
past 20 or 30 years have been his friends and acquaint-
ances, the world is as a shambles. Just think what has
become of all the actors and actresses we have known !
Frank Weston, of the Wizard He, and King-of-Sardinia
moustachios, turns up again on the Melbourne pav6. He
was returned as killed in Africa some time ago, but it
seems another Frank Weston, theatrical, goes about
obligingly getting drowned, or assegaied, in his stead.
Over 20 years since Frank debuted in a white ooat,
digging down and speaking a piece, over his Wizard Oil,
and then he was middleman with Tobias and Biancas
Hussey, and Big Sunflower Harry Kelly, on the corners.
It is some triumph to have outlived such men. A few
bottles still on hand.
There was great antagonism between Mrs. Kendal, as
Madge Robertson, and Walter Montgomery. It arose at
Nottingham while Montgomery managed there, and some
thought Madge had refused him. Out of the company
Walter picked no less than live other Juliets, to his
Romeo, in a desperate attempt to go one better than
Madge, but failed. Two days before his marriage, and
four days before his suicide, he wrote to an actor named
Sinclair that he was the happiest man on earth.
Government House, Brisbane,
February 20,1891.
Herbert Flemming, Esq., Theatre Royal.
Sir,—ln reply to your letter of 19th instant, I have to
inform you that Sir Arthur Palmer will not be present
this evening at the Theatre Royal.
His Excellency, having read the book on which the
play “ Camille ” is written from, objects to be present
with ladies at a play of its character.
I am, sir, your obedient servant,
W. 11. D. Palmer, A.D.C.
The moral of this grammatical epistle is apparent: If a
thing—whether it be play, lecture, picture, or statue-be
moral, artistic, and good of its kind, it does not require
the imprint of the patronage of any man whose official
position has not been given to him because of his fitness
to pass judgment on such matters, but for wholly differ-
ent reasons. Patronage of this kind has a tendency to
secure temporary popularity for trashy wares, vended by
those who are keen and cute in self-advertisement to the
neglect of the more artistic and healthy productions of
those who follow Art almost as much for love of the beau-
tiful as for monetary gain and notability. It would be no
bad thing if gubernatorial snubs of this character became
frequent. The visits of a Governor to a theatre should be
unheralded and unproclairned, as private as those of any
ordinary citizen. Sensible men would prefer it. The
Governor whose back in the dress-circle is made an adver-
tising-stand on which to stick the bill of the play lowers
whatever official dignity his office may possess, and thosa
who invite him debase, to the same degree, their art, and
besmudge the true value of their histrionic goods.

A London audience promptly resented the burlesque of
John Burns addressing dock-strikers, introduced in the
new extravaganza “Joan of Arc.” This was hissed clean
off the stage. Theatrical managers, and newspaper ones,
always try the wrong end of the stick first.
Jones’s play, “The Dancing Girl,” is apparently booked
for a twelvemonth’s run in London. It is Frenchy in
suggestion, but original, with its Quaker Phryne. Jones
bounds to the first position amid the dramatists, for this
play is held to be an advance even on Pinero’s “Profligate.”
England makes haste slowly to her Dumases and Sardous.
Johnny Sheridan|opens at Sydney Garrick in “ Bridget
O’Brien, Esq.a continuation of the “ Widdy” story, in
which that interesting female shines as a mother-in-law.
G. R. Ireland and Co. get heaps of jam from the Broken
Hill press for their “Silver King” performance. So does
Artist James Peake, once of Sydney Royal.

A London paper invites dramatists to look around
them, and consider such magnificent situations as are
afforded by Parnell and Bismarck Especially that scene
between Empress Frederick and Bismarck, whether real
or imagined by Blowitz signifies not, it is so intensely
dramatic. It would bring down a curtain almost as well
as Louise of Lome and her Bohemian Boehm.
In the old days of the Victorian drama Inglewood was
reckoned the fatal place, for here died Miss Herbert and
Messrs. F. Webster and Graham, over whose grave
M‘Kean Buchanan impressively read the funeral service,
with all the Thespians around.
Macready writes, in his autobiography, of the most
curious actress he ever met, Mrs. Whitlock. Though a
veteran, she was truly fascinated with the stage, and
Mac. listened to her with wonder. Charles Wheatleigh,
who brought “Colleen Bawn ” and “Shaughraun” to
Australia, was also an actor who never cared to move off
the stage.
Lola Montez was acting at the Bendigo Theatre in a
drama called “The Little Devil,” when the stage was
actually struck by real lightning and a plank shivered,
whereupon Lola turned coolly to the manager, exclaiming
“ I never saw this piece put on so realistically anywhere,
and please accept my compliments for your admirable
stage effects.”
Miss Macintyre, prima donna of Sullivan’s “Ivanhoe,”
enthuses to an interviewer on the lordly style of doing
things at D’Oyly Carte’s new Royal Opera House.
Luncheon is even provided at rehearsal, a thing Miss
Mac. never before heard of.
Downey, the aristocratic photographer, declares that
never a photo, went off like Kate Vaughan’s. Royal
personages from everywhere scooped them up by the
bushel. He takes photos, of Letty Lind, Sylvia Grey and
Phyllis Broughton actually while dancing, the instruction
being—“Please dance up to that wild rose on the carpet.”
The tripod and lens are all ready, and on goes the cap.
Betty’s best selling photo, is a mere heap of clothes on
the carpet, with one leg visible.
Cagnotte has made a Balzackian and Zolaistio study of
a rich lady who married an opera artist in the full tide of
his success, and took him off the stage. Most amusing to
note how she keeps him out of mischief, like a hen with
one duck. The poor fellow’s superb basso is quite chained
up, and he can only make the walls of her boudoir vibrate
with “II Balen.” But a brilliant prima donna, living in
Melbourne, has for a series of years sacrificed herself to
her husband.

Australian lecturing tour.

Prances Saville will receive a farewell concert at Sydney
Town Hall, March 21, prior to her departure for England,
where her husband, Max Rown, intends to establish a
head-centre for Australian agencies, and where the fair
lady herself will most likely create a sensation. Provi-
dence has not always been so bountiful. Your good-
looking woman has usually a voice resembling the screech
of a Sioux.

Australian appearance.

Tales of the Old Regime.
No. II.—The Rationing of the Sentinels.
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
We told you* how Convict Jerricks' mother
carried unto Convict Jerricks’father —Dr. Rossell,
Commandant at Port Arthur—a clay-cast taken
from Convict Jerricks’ shoulders as he lay dead ih
his coffin.
This is the sequel to that tale. It is a tale by
itself, because we should be doing a gross injustice
to the Regime were we to leave 'undescribed a
single incident of the series which flowed from the
making of the death-mask. A convict’s kindly
act nearly always brought its Nemesis in the epoch
bf thfe Regime.
* * *
“Wud yer like a death-mask; a clay-
picter ? ” had asked Convict Symm, senior-grave-
digger at Dead Man’s Isle, of the Widow Jerricks.
“I ’udn’t do it ’cept for a mother. Anybody else
’lid split;”
There was no King's Regulation against the
manufacture of death-masks that Convict Symm
had ever heard of, but he was quite sure the men
in power would not approve of his attempt to give
consolation.to the poor, stricken woman who had
come down to the Settlement to clasp the living
form of her son in her arms, and had been ushered
to his corpse. The System did not, as a matter
of simple fact, approve of anything in the shape
of consolation being administered to any of its
suffering children. The Secretary of State for
the Colonies, who ordered that transports must
tear outof their hearts all the roots uf domestic love
and friendly affection, epitomised in a sentence
the policy which controlled the Regime. Convict
Symm, being a transport of some years’ standing,
was aware of the determination of the authorities
to stamp out the sanctities of human intercourse,
and had, therefore, as he- imagined, guarded
against the news of the death-mask reaching Com-
mandant Rossell’s ears.
“No mother ’ud split," was his thought, and no
doubt he was right in thinking that no woman
would ever consciously bring the man who had
procured her a memorial of her dead child to
punishment for his gracious deed.
Unfortunately, however, for Convict Symm,
when the Widow Jerricks showed the “clay
picter,” with its deep-sunk lines that so faithfully
reflected the ridges which the lash had raised on
her son’s back, to Dr. Rossell, her son’s father, she
forgot she was-also submitting it for the inspection
of Dr. Rossell, Commandant at Port Arthur, and
therefore to an official who was bound by every
legal and moral obligation to severely punish ia
prisoner who was so grossly insubordinate as to
convey Government property to a civilian.
As to the putative father of Convict Jerricks,
Dr. and Commandant Rossell shuddered when he
was shown the clay impression of the marks which
his fatal floggings had left upon his son’s shoulders.
He never for a moment doubted Mrs. Jerricks’
assertion that the young transport was his son by
her. The thing was so very probable. But, while
he shuddered, he recollected that he was Com-
mandant, and that as Commandant lie had a
solemn duty to perform.
It has ever been the proud boast of the British
administrator that at the call of Duty, self in his
case slinks out of sight. He will, if need, emulate
the Brutus, and lacerate his father’s heart to do
his duty to his country.
Commandant.-Rossell ordered Convicts Symm
and Mowbray to be brought before him on an
accusation of “delivering to a civilian Govern-
ment property within their charge."
On the Tuesday it was that the death-mask
nad been taken. On the Wednesday morning
Boat’s Crew No. 1 conveyed to Dead Island, in
addition to a corpse, a convict constable and two
men’s rations.
The grave-diggers—Symm, senior, and Mow-
bray, junior—were awaiting the arrival of the
boat. They had carried the bier to the strip of
shingly beach which the incoming tide was
petting gently and fringing with a lacework of
foam, and sitting upon it, waited, counting the
beat of the oars.
, “Two !” exclaimed young Mowbray. “That’s
a bit of bad luck, Symm—we’ve only six open
now. ” It was a rule that six graves at least should
be open every morning, in case of emergencies and
tushes of business.
“’Sh, ’Any !” answered his mate. “They’re
coinin’ too easy-like for two. It’s one and a pas
senger. When you’ve heard ’em as frequent as
me, lad, know as they can’t out the water
as clean as she’s comin now when there’s a brace
aboard. They can’t trim her so nice.”
“ ’Nother passenger? I hope not, Jim. Mark
my words, Jim, if ’tis, ’tis a sneakin’ cull J with a
jark || over that bit o’ clay yesterday. I’m afeard
we’ll be ’fore th’ Com’dant yet over that bizness.”
The boat laboured into sight. In the stern sat
a man wearing a straw hat and the blue-jean
jacket of the convict-constabulary uniform. Its
right arm was akimbo, and from its fingers
dangled a pair of handcuffs which glittered in the
sunlight.
“ ’Arry ’’—and Jim Symm panted painfully as
lie spoke, “yer right. That d woman as
split. I ’udn’t ’a’ thought it of a mother. But,
’Arry, yer don’t know nothink about that ’ere
mask. I’m on’y to blame. D’ yer see? ”
“ But, Jim, it’s a floggin’ matter. An’ I’m in it
a,s well as you.'’
“ Shot up, yer fool.” Jim Symm dropped his
voice to a whisper ; a dozen strokes would beach
the boat. “If yer in it, they won’t divide the
cuts, but’ll tie hup th’ pair of us.”
“ Rasy all—hold water —in oars—damn it, No. 3,
wot d’ye mean wash in’ the stiff ’un?”
No. 3, taking in his oar, has spooned some water
on the nameless coffin ; but returns the coxswain’s
rebuke by remarking with a chuckle
“Thought I’d baptise him, cox’en. You’ll bo
sure, sex’on, ho don’t go into unconsecrated
ground.”
The boat nosed the pebbled margin ; the grating
of her keel and a burst of ribald laughter sounded
the nameless transport’s requiem, as his coffin was
slid over the side and dropped with a “ woosh ”
on the beach.
The sextons were moving forward to place it on
the bier when the prisoner-constable stepped before
them.
‘‘Leave it alone for a while, prisoners, Yer've
got ter come along ter the Com’dant.’’
“ Wot for ?” demanded Symm.
“Wot the as that ter do with you?
Com’dant’s orders, that’s wot ’tis.”
“My orders are not to leave ‘Dead ’Tins’
without seein’of a written border,” said Symm,
firmly.
“I’m yer lorful s’pefior, ain’t I? Yer refuse
ter obey me at yer peril.”
“I won’t unless I see yer warrant.”
The constable turned to the boat’s crew.
- “In the King’s flame, ineu, yer ’elp—in-
subordiriet pris’ner.”
The men rose; there was fad resisting a call of
the kind even when issued sneaking cull.”
To have wavered in response was to hate lost their
comfortable billets.
“ Without a warrant ” —Symm had beguh a
fresh remonstrance, when jocose and loyal No; 3
struck him with his oar and brought him to the
water, into which he fell, face - downwards,
senseless.
“In the King’s name, Symm - an’ I owe yer one
a-cause yer wudvi’t give mb a smoke.” Several of
the creW laughed.
The younger sexton rushed iiltd the water and
lifted his mate’s head. The constable grasped
Symm’s legs, and then, with
A He,
And a Ho !
And a heave, heave, Ho !
swung his share of the inanimate burden into the

drop that.”
“ Drop wot ? ”
“ The ’cad o’ that insubordinet pris’ner.”

“COULDN’T SHE?”
SHE (a good little woman with a separate estate, who would sell her soul for the old man)
“And now, dear, as we are so hard-up, why shouldn’t I mortgage my house
and raise some money?”
HE: “But then your mother lives in it, and if it were foreclosed on she’d be turned
out.”
SHE: “Well, suppose so; then she could come and live with us, couldn’t she?”
The coffin lay where it had fallen. Fifty yards
away a point of ashen shagreen, a broad pectoral
fin, rose from the curvature of a wave; further
out still, marking its sentinel path in huge evolu-
tionary circles a hideous hammer-head; and gulls,
in light-winged squadrons, hung over the surface,
innumerable, discordant, predatory.
At the pier steps the constable displayed
a not uncommendable anxiety to restore the senior
grave-digger to his senses. It was easting an
insult upon Justice for an offender to present him-
self at the Commandant’s Court with his wits
gone a wool-gathering, and therefore the officer
dashed a full bucket of water upon the recal-
citrant wretch, and then another, and finally a
third. Quivering in every muscle, with biting
and frothing of lips, with convulsive writhiugs
of the body and limbs so violent in their epileptic
force that Mowbray—to whom his mate was still
manacled—felt as though his arm were being
pulled from its socket, Convict Symm at last
awoke to a painful perception of his duty.
They pulled him to his feet, Mowbray swaying
with his every movement and partially supporting
him.
“ Water !” he craved.
“Ain’t yer had ’nuff?” grinned the minion of
the law, “ We’ve jest been a-givin’ of yer a good
lot.”
“ Water. For God’s sake, water !”
“We’ve left yer rashing over at ‘Dead tin’s.’
D’yer wan’ ter break the Systum with yer
extravagance? Yer sh’u’dn’t ’a’ come out a-
pleasurin’ so early, an’ then yer ’udn’t ’a’ gone
without yer rashing.”
“ Look ’ere, cox’n, give Symm my ratun for to-
morrow, will yer ? Oh, do, sir, do ! Yer can
stop it, yer know, w’en yer bring it across ! ”
Mowbray thus pleaded.
In the boat was kept a small water-keg, each
man of the crew being allowed, When ih service,
ah extraalldwanceofthtefluid. Theeoxswain,moved
by Mowbray’s appeal, would have granted the
request had the constable not interposed.
“ Yer do, cox’n, an’ I’ll tepoft yer. Yer know
the Reg’lashin about billet-men showih’ favours
to pris’hers. Jest take a fr’en’s advice, an’ leave
theste chaps ter me; Yer ain’t no call ter inter-
fere. I'm responsible.”
As he said these words, the constable thrust out
his chest and touched it; as if tb draw attention to
the fact that he was inspired by the tlirice-sacred
afflatus of office. And the raging thirst of the
poor devil went unquenched.

“James Symm, No. 739, Malabar, and
Harry or Henry Mowbray, alias Deans, No. 1515,
Katherine Stewart Forbes, second trip, charged
with appropriating, On the 3rd April instant,
Government property, to wit, two pounds of clay,
entrusted to their charge as sextons on Isle de
Morts, and disposing of same to a civilian, ohe
Catherine Jerricks, free by servitude, ship name
Catherine Higgins—how d’you plead I”
The two prisoners were before that Rhadaman-
thine officer, Dr. and Commandant Rossell. In-
vested with high magisterial powers of a degree
subordinate only to those exercised by the
Supreme Court Judge, the Police Magistrate at
Hobart Town, and the Principal Superintendent
of Convicts—the title of Comptroller-General did
not come into official use till a year or two later—
he sat in his office throned in the majesty of his
littleness, and in the virtue of a practically un-
limited authority. Men came into his presence
cowering, who had lifted unabashed foreheads to
the magnificent humbug of an Assize Court, and
left it liable to punishments unknown to the
latter and foreign to the spirit of English statute-
law of the time, Draconian though it was. Yet
among the two thousand odd sentences passed by
him, it would be difficult, if not impossible, to
point out one which exceeded the discretionary
latitude permissible to him as a servant of the
State, to whom constitutional authority had
delegated enormous powers. Place in the hand
of any average man the Transportation Acts of
George 111. and IV. and the successive codes of
Kings’ Councils and Local Regulations; give him,
in the other, a commission “of discretionary
capacity ”; allot to him sovereignty, absolute in
all save in name, over the lives and destinies of a
thousand or two fellow-creatures who have been
deprived alike of all civil rights and of all the
influences of social privileges; sequester him and
them from the eye of the stranger; and you will
produce, first, a functionary like Dr. and Com-
mandant Rossell; and, second, a condition of
society which no voice dare speak and no pen
. describe, but which, notwithstanding, is para 1-
leled in the history of a dozen Australasian
localities. A Frankenstein created one monster :
the Regime Created many, sbme of whom tran-
scended in quality of terribleness the monster of
fiction by so much as an actual horror always
exceeds an ideal one. This diatribe has a purpose;
* * *
If he were a monster Dr. Rossell was
rather a pleasant-looking one as he appeared
seated at his desk with his round, florid face, his
hands, and his well-knit form so nattily set
off by his laced shirt and blue-uniform coat. But
we have Hamlet’s word for it that the Devil has
power to assume a pleasing shape, and the
amiability of the Commandant’s demeanour was
not incompatible with a devotion to the best in-
terests of the System.
“ What do yoii plead ? ” repeated Rossell im-
patiently. , “ Yoii’ve hbard the charge—what d’ye
say to it ? ”
“ Know nbthing ’bout it, sir I ” answered Mow-
bray. “ Don’t know what it means. I never ’lid
no prqputy of Gov’ment that any woman ’ud
want. ”
(< ‘‘We’ll see about that,” qfioth the Bench.
You plead not guilty. Are yoh Symm or Mow-
bray ? ”
“ Mowbray, sin He’s Symm, and, sir, he’s bad
“Hold-your tongue, Pris’ner Mowbray. Let
him speak for himself. Plead, Symm—guilty or
not guilty?”
Symm looked stupidly at the Bench. With rt
swollen tongue that hardly consented to perform
its office, he responded, “Water, sir.” The words
were mumbled, and it was no Wonder Rossell
misunderstood them, and considered that a current
cant phrase had been levelled at him.
“ Walker ! Walker I ” His insulted dignity
flamed out in sudden rage. “ I’ll Hookey Walker
you ! Warder Brewer, take him out and give him
a dozen. ”
Some constables stepped out of their rank to
obey the order, but Mowbray, forgetting for the
moment that he w T as still manacled to his brother-
convict, had that instant propelled himself
towards Rossell’s desk, crying “No, no! Beg
pardon, sir, but he’s bad, sir—be arsked for water
—he’s had a fit, sir.” Symm had yielded mechani-
cally to Mowbray’s rush forward, but the rude
hands of the convict-officers hauled him back by
the shoulders. Between the two, so sudden a
jerk was inflicted on the shivering unfortunate as
to once more drive him into epileptic convulsions.
He fell on the floor, pulling his mate down also,
and beat the planks with Ids head and feet. Not
a single creature of all the anointed scoundrelism
that pervaded the P. A. Hall of Justice, from the
Magistrate-Commandant to the newest “sneaking
cull, '’ but was convinced the man was really and
terribly ill. The force of habit, however, sug-
gested that the Commandant should request of
Mustering-Warder Brewer ;
“ Sure he isn’t shamming Abram, Brewer?”
A sudden writhe —a doubling of the body—a
bulging of great veins—and Mr. Brewer was
saved the trouble of answering, for Symm had
broken the bracelet which held him to liis fellow.
A severance of the handcuffs, “Y.D.L. No. 2B ”
pattern, was impossible to any man not endowed
with the superhuman strength which sometimes
nerves the epileptic and the lunatic. This Rossell
clearly recognised.
“Remove the pris’ner,” he said, “hospital,
temporarily. Never mind that ‘ dozen,’ Brewer.”
Brewer sainted in acknowledgment of the sur-
passing leniency which had prompted the remis-
sion of the 25 lashes. A “ dozen ” was always 25.
when it was a matter of cat-cuts. When it was a
ease of prisoners’ rations, it was usually 9 or 10.
The excess in the one case balanced the deficiency
in the other.
“ Now, Mowbray, you’ve pleaded not guilty,”
resumed His Honor. “But, of my own know-
ledge I know you or Symm was guilty. I saw the
clay—-I know it came from the graveyard—and
there was only one of you to give it to—ah, the
lady. Now, what have you to say to the charge ?”
The young sexton was almost anew hand. *By
one of these bits of luck which Dame Fortune
occasionally dropped from her overflowing horn
he had arrived at the Port just as a vacancy had
occurred in the junior diggership. A nine-
months’ voyage in that lazar-ship, the Katherine
Stewart Forbes (2), had changed his bone to
gristle and his flesh to skin. Hence he had been
appointed to the sextonship, which demanded
enormous muscular strength for its due fulfil-
ment. Such billets were all but invariably filled
in accordauce with the principle of the eternal
unfitness of things.
Nevertheless Harry Mowbray had not died.
Symm, his colleague in the grim function,
encouraged, helped him, amused him and kept
him alive. Here and there in the penal records
is to be traced the history of an old convict who
did not do his best to drive a young ’un to a lower
deep of infamy. Symm was such a one. He had
kept Harry alive, comparatively innocent—and
independent.
It was the last quality that prompted the
prisoner to retort upon the Commandant, with a
contemptuous merriment ringing out in the
syllables of the daring speeeh :
“ It’s the lump of mud wo made the mask of,
yer botherin’ of, is it? That’s Government
proputty, is it; Gawd ! yer Honor, I can’t help
a-larfing to think of this fuss about a bit o’ clay !”
Except for the echoes of his o-wn laughter, the
Court was silent. To thus beard the Commandant
to his face! ’Twas folly—rank madness! Then—
“By the Lord, pris’ner! I’ve known magis-
trates, who had they been sitting here, would
have hanged you for those words !” roared the
Bench. “You insolent scoundrel! Fifty for
insolence!”
Mowbray was forced into a now frightened
quietude. “ I didn’t mean no gab, yer Honor ”
“ Silence ! Who gave the woman the clay and

made the thing she called the mask —yourself or
Symm? Answer, prisoner.”
Symm had done both. The death-cast was,
indeed, Symm’s suggestion. But the prisoner
thought, in for a penny, in for a pound. He was
due for 50 -why - should he not take the dose
which Symm was certain to get if his share in the
transaction became known? Symm had been
kind to him--had consoled him when the home
sickness had shattered him with its misery—had
divided —O, supreme height of self-denial ! his
rare morsels of baccy with him. Reflecting on
these things, he resolved to lie. And lie he did—
bravely. .
“Answer, prisoner, or else 111 treat you as
f ’ U ‘ ‘ Treat me as you damned please. X did it, if
you want to know. Symm wouldn’t have any-
th ink to do with it—kicked up a row because I
guv the woman, curse her, the marsk. ”
“You’re getting along, Fifteen-fifteen. I’m
accustomed to do what you term my damned
pleasure, and I propose to do it in your ease.
What did you do it for? Mopuses?”
* * Xq—l did it because she said she was the
dead ’tin’s mother. I didn’t think a mother would
liaVe split. Not : a mother—or a father—anybody
t lse might—that’s what I thought—an’—one, the
mother did arter all.” .
The mother—or the father ? The conscience of
the official man at the desk aroused itself for a
second and tortured him. Had the woman Jcr-
ooks told the gravedigger of the paternity of the
dead convict? The indurated feelings of a gen-
tleman, even of the Regime, shrank from Jetting
„o unpleasant a fact become a theme of Settlement
gossip. As a man of courage, however, he would
face the doubt and fight it.
“So the woman was deceased’s mother, eh ?
And who was his father, did you hear ? ” Rossell
spoke with quite a pleasant humour, as though he
w as passing a little joke.
fn for a lamb as he was, Convict Mowbray,
other wise Deans, thought he might go the whole
sheep. No knowledge of the actual state of
aff airs; no suspicion even, nothing but sheer devil-
ment, prompted his retort.
“No, ’ow should I know? P’r’aps, yerself, yer
Honor—l ain’t been ’ere long, so 1 can’t say for
certain, but there's a "ole ’eap of orficers’ sons
doin’ time, I’m told. § You’ll know better’n me,
sir.”
That was the climax. The nasty, aggravating
chuckle accentuated the words, and the stung
sensibilities of the now maddened Commandant
nursed the insult as though his parental responsi-
bility for the dead derricks were patent to the
whole world.
The tw r o gravediggers were in company together
again that day. Before nightfall the junior had
joined the senior in the hospital. They occupied
adjoining beds—No. 12—Symm ; No. 14, Mow-
bray. Bed No. 14’s last tenant was one derricks,
since departed to “ Dead Man’s.”
* * *
In the silence of the midnight, an hour
when the gloom of the hospital ward took an in-
tenser tinge of awfulness from the memories and
legends which would cling to the rows of pallets,
No. 12 whispered to his neighbour.
“ ’Arry, lad, are you ’wake ? ”
“ Aye, Jimmy, I'm awake !” was the moaned
response. “ I wish I wasn’t—l wish I—was—
’sleep—for ever.”
“Don’t ye, now, lad, don’t ye 1 I’m so sorr>,
’Arry—it’s all my fault-all my—fault, sonny
A great sigh completed the sentence, and gained
for answer a gasping moan.
“ Did it ’urt yer much—Arry ? ”
“Orful—l ’erd the ’underd tally—don’t know
no more -’twas two ’underd I was tied—up
for .” Another groan.
“Poor ’Arry—an’ my fault! 0 Gawd !”
“Not yer fault, dim ! The mother’s.”
“ A mother to split, ’Arry ! Who’d ha’ thought
it, lad?”
“Blast her, Jim !—blast her !”
“Amen !”
# # #
An hour later. The wardsman’s night-
light flickered more feebly in the draught from an
open window; the wardsman snored more pro-
foundly. A sleeper in one of the “ odd numbers ”
turned restlessly and sobbed “ Wife !”
“ Jim !”
“ ’Arry ?”
“ That dead ’un—this mornin’s boat —you
'member ?”
“Yes,’Arry!”
“ Would—they send —others —to bury ’im ?”
A feeble laugh. Then—
“No, lad—they’ll wait till—we’re better.”
“ An’ if we didn’t get—better ?”
“The ’ammer-’eads ’ud take ’im. Thera sharks
’ll be uf> with the ’igh tide.”
“ Two o’clock, and All’s Well!” The tramp of
the patrol and the cry of their corporal awoke the
wardsman for a brief space. He lifted his whistle
to his lips, blew his response, and before the sound
of the marching feet had died away was slumber-
ing once more.
“ Jim ?”
“ ’Arry?”
“ Them sharks —they get rashings, don’t they?”
“ Reg’lar, an’ all cross-readers* as well. If you
an’ me were to kill ourselves, we’d be dropped
into Long Bay. The Com’dant caljs ’em his best
sent’nels.”
# * #
Half-past two. A hand protrudes itself
from No. 14 bed —the effort causing its owner to
groan subduedly—and touches the occupant of
No. 12, who is, at last, sleeping.
“Jim.”
“ Yes, sir.” Symm starts up, and brings his
hand to his forehead in salute.
“ Jim, it’s only me.”
“Oh, ’ivrry —thought’twas theCom’dant. Wot
is it.”
“ I’m agoin’, Jimmy, to them sharks.”
A pause.
Harry waited—not daring to express the hope
that had arisen within him. But Jim understood.
“ I’ll go with you, ’Arry—to ration—the ol’
man’s best senthels.”
The hands met in the space between the beds—
and clasped,
* * *
They waited till after “ three o’clock and
all’s well ” had gone by, and the return signal had
been whistled. And, then, when the stertorous
breath of the wardsman told of his heavy slum-
ber, they rose, feeling neither pain or exhaustion
in the exaltation of their resolve, and, Jim’s hand
to Harry’s back, moved step by step down the
room.
At the end were hanging on the wall at either
side of the open window two huge printed
placards : “ The Grace —Before and after Meat,”
one; the other, “Abstract of Hospital Regula-
tions.”
Harry tried to recall a prayer. He could not
do so ; and so, not daring, as they were close to
the wardsman, to again whisper to Symm, placed
his hand reverently on what he thought was the
“ Grace ” card.
The act was the Viaticum of his untutored soul;
nor dare we think that it will lose its savor, be-
cause in the dim and flickering light he made a
mistake as to the card.
*. # *
From the quarry-bed, overhung by the
hospital-window from which they had leapt, their
remains were removed next day to —the Sentinels.
PRICE WARUNG.
Three separate Chinese doctors practise in
Fitzroy (Melb.), and all live in much style.
Having the same surname, they appear to be
members of a family.
The Siberia railway—Vladivostock to St.
Petersburg—just begun, will be of a length,
beyond existing Russian railways, of 4810 miles,
and will cost 30 millions.

only.”

Bega (N.S.W.) Gazette recently de-
pastured in its columns a milch cow yielding daily
601 b. of milk, and said to be “only eight months
old.” Later, it corrected the romance by averring
that it meant “ eight months calved .” The correc-
tion is almost as “ steep ” as the first perjury.
Dan. O’Connor “ I have known no
better men and no better women, no people
whose charitable works have acquired a more
widely-extended fame, or who have reflected more

the licensed victuallers of the colony, ” Paul, the
Apostle; Paul, the greasy humbug—“all things
to all men.”
About five years ago an Oamaru (N.Z.)
man lost a leg to a shark while bathing on the
local beach. A man on horseback rescued the
rest of him at the time, and he now goes about
with a wooden leg. The other day he was bathing
again at the same place, when the same shark
came round to collect the other limb. This time,
however, the Oamaruffian, on seeing his ancient
enemy, turned round quickly on his back, and
gave the monster of the deep a paralysing dig in
the ribs with his wooden leg. On recovering
somewhat, the shark turned tail and fled into
deeper water. The moral is obvious.

Good news from Portugal : A Republican
rebellion has broken out there, which, according
to cable, “ the Government are utterly powerless
to suppress.”
Said Bismarck lately:—“The chemist is
the only man who can answer the war question.
Whatever enemy of ours gets the absolutely best
powder first will give the signal for attack.”
The members of Parliament on the N.S. W.
Labour Commission are the only ones who do not
attend the meetings of the Commission regularly.
They are the only members who are not paid to
attend.

will have the effect among those who witness them

i wish every Australian to understand is that, if

The Melbourne Trades and Labour Council
has asked the Victorian Premier to see that, when
plural voting is abolished, every member must be
returned by a majority of the electors who record
their suffrages at the poll. The Bux.letin claims
the credit of this suggestion.
G. H. Reid refuses to draw his official
salary as a N.S.W. M.P. In justification of his
refusal it may be said that he does not work in
Parliament except about once or twice a year,
when lie turns up and makes a splash with a
speeh he has written out and learnt by rote.
Says G. H. Reid “ There are men (in
Parliament) who would not get a rotten sixpence
in any business concern in the city ; they are men
who would shipwreck the smallest concern in the
universe.” The Bulletin is of opinion that
Parkes has herein the basis of a very good slander-
action.

“It is incumbent upon all the Victorian
delegates at the Federal Convention,” says the
Age, “ to remember that they represent a protec-
tionist colony in any discussion upon fiscal
matters.” Then the Age proceeds to advocate
intercolonial Freetrade with protection against
the outside world.
G. H. Reid is not in favour of Imperial
Federation —not because he wants Australia to
become an independent nation, mark you; but
because he fears—fears is the word—that any
attempt towards achieving Imperial Federation
might precipitate the consummation of the Aus-
tralian Republic ’
John Haynes denounces Wollongong
Stevenson for his “ change of front.” John s front
will never change.
“ One Nation — One Destiny ” is a beauti-
ful Federation banquet toast—but “One Man -
One Vote ” will require attention first.
Sir George Grey says .that the Federal
Convention may do great good or may retard
Federation for many years. True. Federation
is the Koch’s lymph of Australian politics, and
must be administered in infinitesimal doses.
G. H. Reid, seeing that Parkes is quadril-
ling sinuously towards Protection —and a Coalition
Ministry—wishes to squelch the old man. The
big importers of Sydney begin to feel that it is
time to shunt Parkes as dangerous. It may con-
fidently be predicted that the chief result of the
Federation Convention will be to raise, instead of
laying, the N.S.W. fiscal question.

In Brisbane a lot of workmen have been
disqualified as electors because they inserted their
street numbers in the application forms, instead
of the allotment numbers marked on the Govern-
ment plan. Some mean individual in the capi-
talist interest struck this brilliant quibble, and
his objection was upheld. The law is a handy
thing for anybody who has got money.

A proposition has been introduced into
the French Legislature making provision ;
(1) For the suppression of collateral inheritances ; for
the seizure by the State of the property of deceased per-
sons not disposed of by will; (3) for the sale, on instal-
ments of the real estate falling to the State in this
manner, as well as the larger part of the national and
communal domains, to the poorer agriculturists and
workers, on condition of occupancy and use.

tem, arid ' who are know-nothings on all other
points—will completely “ down ” his nominees.

Prof. C. V. Riley, U.S. entomologist for
the Department of Agriculture, has created a
sensation in America by declaring that man’s
position has been attained through millions of
years of sexual selection on the part of the females
of his own race and of the anthropoid apes and
other inferior species from which he has sprung,
and that the institution of artificial sexual equality
would be a mere kicking-down of the ladder by
which he has climbed.
A paragraph that, if read aright, furnishes
a lesson in political economy for West Australian
legislators :
The chairman of the Western Australian Land Company
writes to the Financial News (London) to express the
opinion that the soil (of Western Australia) is good and
virgin, and the climate superb, so that if the new Govern-
ment will only avail themselves of the experience and
errors of the eastern colonies, and borrow and expend
with prudence, a glorious and prosperous future of
Western Australia is close at hand.
In other words, if the W. A. Government borrows
and spends with prudence, a glorious and pros-
perous future awaits the absentee shareholders in
the W. A. Land Co.

ticket, and it scorns to ask favours from the
plausible old sneak who is so notoriously addicted
to wreaking paltry revenges on his critics.
Parkes, G.C.M.G., cannot patronise the Repub-
lican Bulletin, which, though its conductors
have, for the most part, been ccmtent to work in
the obscurity of a newspaper-office, • has made a
lot of real Australian history.
Apropos of Lord Londonderry, the Evictor-
General, 20,000, and more, brawny, right arms are
(cable) raised in rebellion, because this big iron
master, this Brassey, turns out his ..Silksworth
tenants, demanding implicit obedience, on the
basis of a ukase which the Londonderry of 1837
issued to all his slaves in Durham, making a sen-
sation all through England. The Marquis and
Marchioness of Londonderry of that period joined
in a circular to all their tenantry, commanding
them to vote for a Conservative M.P., on pain of
being evicted. Wise they were, those Castle-
reaghs, who saw the storm coming. What a
charming historic name in Britain is Castlereagh,
the man whose very dead remains were groaned
by a London mob, at the funeral!
Wolfe’s Schnapps will do you no harm.
Wolfe’s Schnapps will make you a new man.

Society
A member of N.S.W; Government House
staff intimated to a pressman the other day that
Governor Jersey’s name should be pronounced as
spelt; and not “Jarsey.” It appears that a lot of
people speak of the Earl as Jarsey, and he “don’t
like it, you know.” “Derby ” is “Darby;” why
isn't “ Jersey ” “ Jarsey?”
* # *
The Stkte-scliool children of Penrith,
N.S.W., were compelled to go through the farce
of presenting Governor Jersey with a complimen-
tary address on the occasion of his wasting a
bottle of bad fizz and turning on a water-cock the
other day. This complimentary address was read
by a boy and presented by two girls, but it was
siened by teachers “on behalf of the teachers and
scholars.” This disgusting and bogus document
is, of course, “ another striking evidence of the
loyalty of the native Australians to the throne and
person ”of V.R. Here it is -
While loving well Australia, our native land, we are
also proud of Britain, our glorious mother-land. Your
Excellency’s kindly presence here to-day is a new link in
the chain which binds us to the throne of Victoria, our
sweet and gracious Queen.
Here insert the latest fancy portrait of “our
sweet and gracious Queen.”
* # #
The indispensable condition of our recent
visitor young Lord Dudley’s aspiring to the hand
of the Brince of Wales’ daughter is that he shall
“retire from business.” What a commentary on
the grand cardinal principle of Society, even in
Australia, that Labour is Dishonourable, and work
Disgraceful! Yet the line of Dudley would have
gone extinct but for business, 200 years ago. The
broken-down Earl married the rich daughter and
heir of plain Master Humble Ward, the King’s
Jeweller.
* # *
We are told that a rule nisi for divorce
granted in camera six months ago to a more or
less distinguished N.S.W. politician was made
absolute last week—also in camera. According
to this there is a law for the rich and influential
and a law for the poor and uninfluential. Also
there is a sup-Bress for the rich and influential.
* * *
Myles M‘Rae, the burly, uncouth member
for Morpeth in the N.S.W. Assembly, was neatly
“had ”in the Divorce Court. Mrs. M‘Rae had peti-
tioned fur a divorce, and the giddy Mac, no doubt
thinking that he was going to be made quite free,
offered no defence —whether he could have made
a defence in any case we are not aware. The
petitioner, in support of her claim, tendered all
sorts of damning evidence ; Myles had beaten her
in the street; had seduced a girl under
promise of marriage, saying that he had divorced
his wife; had also been guilty of a number of
other disgraceful acts, having, for instance, gone
about naked in her (Mrs. M'Rae’s) house in the
presence of the servants. She added that though
she had been married to him for 20 years, she had
not cohabited with him for more than four years
in all. These facts having been put in evidence
practically without cross-examination, Mrs.
M‘Rae’s adviser suddenly requested that as the
petitioner, being a Roman Catholic, could not
conscientiously accept a divorce, a judicial separa-
tion should be granted. This was agreed to by
the judge, and the bold, bad Myles, who is under-
stood to aver that he didn’t defend simply because
he wanted to be divorced, lias still all the definite
and indefinite responsibilities of a married man.
* * #
Myles M‘Rae, M.L.A., was constantly in
and out of the N.S.W. Divorce Court during the
hearing of the suit for judicial separation brought
by his wife, and appeared to derive considerable
entertainment from the evidence. His “victim ”
(Miss Vaughan) is a pretty girl, with a most
attractive svelte figure, and a lovely head of
golden-brown hair. She gave her evidence in a
most matter-of-fact fashion, and certainly did not
appear to “feel her position keenly.” She is
sweet seventeen.
* * *
It is not the Ashmead-Bartlett who married
the venerable Baroness Burdett-Coutts who has,
as cabled, got publicly into trouble with a woman.
That enterprising youth changed his name, and
is known as Mr. “Burdett-Coutts.” The man
now in a tight place is Burdett-Coutts’ brother,
Ashmead-Bartlett, who is a junior member of
the Salisbury Ministry, and runs a paper which
used to be called England. This paper, which
is now called The Beople, has, or had a few years
ago (we have not seen it lately) a tolerable circu-
lation. Bartlett’s troubles will be a godsend to
Barnell, for no doubt Bartlett has made much
Tory political capital by commenting, as is his
wont, virtuously and in most scathing terms on
the social iniquities of the Irish leader. As
already stated, Burdett-Coutts, “husband” of the
barren Baroness, who is old enough to be his
great-grandmother, is not yet in trouble, but if he
is built like ordinary men, he probably will be
Some of these fine days. We are sorry, for his
reputation as a man, that he isn’t in trouble
already.
# * *
The French Club* Sydney, which, alas,
bonsisted in its latter days chiefly of German
gentlemen With enormous appetites, has been
wound up with considerable loss to those respon-
sible. From its assets is to krise the Cosmd l
politan Club, a limited concern, capital £2OOO in
£1 shares, which will take over the liabilities of
the old club oh a new system abolishing entrance
fees and receiving as members only those willing
to beedme shareholders and whom other members
consider eligible.
* * k
The N.S.W; National Club has also burst.
We must One day write its history under the title
of “The Grindstone Club.” The primary cause
Of its inception was possibly the fad that a cer-
tain illustrious (we speak more ot less seriously)
gentleman qharrelled with Lis dinner or something
at the Australian Club and determined to run a
show of Lis own. He had (and fortunately still
has) plenty of money, and was prepared to spend
it plentifully in certain directions, and so the
National Club was established with the avowed
object of encouraging Brotection and promoting
social harmony among Broteetionists. But
A JUDICIOUS RESERVE.
LADY J.: “As I’ve only been in the country 36 hours I have hardly yet made up my
mind about your politics.”
“ business ” not being “ brotherhood,” the two
things were utterly incongruous, and so there-
fined old scientist very soon found. He could
furnish a club, but he couldn’t make a home.
Who could not fail to feel sorry for him when a
couple of prosy political bummers droned their
commonplaces into his ears for hours at a stretch ?
“ That pamphlet which I wrote in 1802 ” ; “ that
splendid speech I delivered to the electors in
1847” —what ghastly torture it must all have
been ! And now the Freetrade papers are chaffing
the members about the sale of the club effects—
the “two butter-knives,” “two meat-skewers,”
“ two pickle-forks,” “one pair of lace curtains,”
“four bottles of rum,” “three bottles of gin.”
Three bottles of gin ! How could anybody or
anything be National on only three bottles of gin?
# # #
However, the moribund N.S.W. Free-
trade and Liberal Association can’t afford to poke
borak at the defunct National. For years past
the former has sponged on Mayor Bnrdekin, who
has found it in board-and-lodging, and, if the
truth were known, probably in washing also. The
secretary has resigned—chiefly, no doubt, because
there was no money to pay him, and The
Bulletin is not aware if the members have in
their corporate capacity even one butter-knife or
three bottles of gin to their name.
* * *
We have not heard anything lately about
Rupert Oarington’s prospective marriage. Beople
who know Rupert well aver that he is so bashful
that he can’t be got to fix the date !
# * *
When Bank-manager Bennett got away so
neatly from Sydney, leaviog behind him a short-
age of £30,000 or so, people marvelled and thought
him wonderfully adroit. Success begets confi-
dence. Bank-manager Bradley, who also leaves
behind a bulky deficit, was not content with
escaping himself, but is believed to have escaped
with his wife and family—he even took a servant
along ! The next absconding bank-manager-mm-
Sunday-school teacher may be expected to elope,
not only with his wife and family and servant,
but with his furniture and the bank’s safe and
premises also, while the fellow after him will kid-
nap all the bank’s reserve bullion and take away
the Board of Directors in a cage! What smart
men are the detectives, if you only tell them all
about it!
# * *
Chesterfield on etiquette says the shoutee
must always deferentially follow the shouter into
the bar, and the correct thing, especially in the
case of policemen, is to glance both up and down
street, then drag in your limbs with evident
langour and unwillingness. Axiom : No man
takes it because he likes it. It is all the work of
tyrant custom.
* * *
Dear Bulletin* —Can you inform me why
the next two statues to be placed in the niches of
the Sydney Lands office are to be those of Burke
and Wills? Beyond the fact of affording matter
for newspaper articles and paragraphs, I really do
not know what distinguished services they ren-
dered New South Wales, or even Australia. In
common justice, the men to occupy the place of
honour, before anybody without exception, should
be Flinders and Sturt, the one representing the
exploration of the coast, the other of the interior.
And as the two men to whose memory Australians
have behaved with the greatest ingratitude, some
slight amends should be made. — Delcojiyn.
[Quite right. The “reason” why Burke and
Wills are “statueised ” and the others neglected
simply is that Sir Henry Barkes, who attends to
all such matters, and will brook no interference,
pretends that he is acquainted with Australian
history, and yetis as ignorant of it as he is of the
technicalities of verse-making.— Ed. B.]

You see nowadays cadet kiddies in khakee
knickerbockers going about Melbourne carrying
their little rifles, and with their little knives stuck
at their sides. Mind you, they all belong to the
Respectable Upper Middle Class, and are drilled
to But Down the Shearers, Lumpers, and Common
Beople, of the next generation.
* * *
The very latest Sydney bank-defaulter is
of the stereotyped pattern—musical and mashing,
pious and priggish. He affected lawn-tennis,
choir-practice, and Sunday-school teaching. A
loudly-proclaimed love for God seems to manufac-
ture embezzlers faster than a proclaimed passion
for riotous living. The subject courts enquiry.
# # *
Says the Master of the Rolls: “I know
hundreds of young men in London who, if they
were told that their trousers were made by begin-
ners, would go mad !” Just so ; we’ve seen ’em.
They walk the streets of Australian cities for
weeks after arrival with their check pants rolled
up at the bottom to reveal their stout boots and
big feet; then they gravitate to the parks and
turn down their hems to hide their battered,
bulgy feet-coverings, and would gladly wear sack-
cloth by way of trousers if it could be got for
nothing, and they fight with unregistered dogs at
midnight among the ashes of dirt-boxes for scraps.
Finally, they’re to be found (if “ wanted ”) clothed
in gill-sodden imbecility, grease, and' patched
pants, peeling potatoes for their tucker in six-
penny restaurants,
# * #
•Justice Foster (N.S. W.) holds open a wide
door for the Salvationists when he decides that
members of the Army cannot legally be charged
with obstructing a thoroughfare “ unless in a
vehicle.” We have the impudence here to remark
that Judge Foster’s decision is not worth a small
bottle of lemonade.
# * #
The habits and customs even of the “ aris-
tocracy ” are changing, and the secret of England’s
greatness, which formerly lay concealed in blue
blood and the Bible, must have shifted its quarters.
Tummy, in his own royal person, has, it is cabled,
himself held an enquiry into the case of a
baronet accused of cheating at cards, and has
sentenced his noble friend to total abstention from
such vanities during the remainder of his natural
life. In the good old days no “ swell ” could cheat
at the gaming table, unless lie were guilty of a
perfectly monstrous act, like the indecent exposure
of fifteen kings up his sleeve. It is told of George
Bayne (who has not been dead ten years) that
when a young greenhorn asked his advice as to
the proper way to deal with an earl whom lie—
the greenhorn—detected in a smart b t of. huffling,
Bayne replied—“ Say nothing, but hack hisplay. ”
The veteran George was sans peur ct sans reproche
as a gambler, but according to the code of club
honour in wdiich he had been trained it was worse
“ form ” to accuse a man of cheating than to cheat
oneself. The proper thing to do was to avoid
playing with parties who enjoyed unnatural
“ luck,” and run no risk of being mixed up in a
“ scene,” So it happens that Queen Victoria, being
well-grounded in the old style of etiquette, tore
around her State apartments and threw the
Imperial crown at the royal tortoiseshell cat when
she heard that her Heir Apparent had assisted at
a row which was worthy only of a pot-house
parlour. The Bulletin doesn't suppose for a
moment that the aforesaid Heir was implicated in
any swindle except as a victim, although nasty
things have evidently been suggested in certain
London papers. Even his intimacy with older
and more experienced card-sharpers than this Sir
William Cumming(anobleman w hose “mug-catch-
ing ” is a common joke amongst the riff-raff of
the racecourse) doesn’t prove anything against
Tummy, seeing that he probably knows less than
other people. No, so long as there is a British
Revenue left to pay his debts, the breath of
rational suspicion will never tarnish Tummy’s
reputation at the card-table. This seems a favour-
able moment for singing the National Anthem.

“MY BOY.”
His mother cries, “ My boy!” and strokes
Her stalwart darling’s hair ;
He merely strolls away and smokes,
And doesn’t seem to care.
Her heart may be as good as gold
Without the least alloy,
Cut Tom is tired of being told
That he’s his mother’s “ boy.”
But when the stars, like little lamps,
Are winking fitful winks,
Along a dusty road he tramps
Beside a skinny minx ;
She’s selfish, plain—a trifle seems
That damsel to annoy,
But yet she fills his thoughts, his dreams,
Because she whines “ My boy ! ”
They marry, and he settles down
Upon a humdrum plan ;
The wife she wears an ugly frown,
And calls him “ My old man.”
This is a world of work and pain,
With little left of joy,
Where nothing makes Tom young again
But mother’s fond “My boy !”
EDMUND FISHER.

* * #
The Gordon-Cumming case reminds us
that many years ago Messrs. Hawksley and
Williamson,, proprietors of the Sydney journal
known as The People’s Advocate, were charged
with libelling Capt. Fitzroy, A.D.O. to his
father the Governor, by publishing an article to
theeffect that the captain had been horsewhipped at
Parramatta by a Mr. Beit for cheating at cards.
The defendants were fined £5 each; and spent six
Weeks in Darlinghurst Gaol.
Tlie whole secret of the Carmelite Nun-
nery trouble at the Warren, Sydney, is just this :
The local founders of the Order arrived in N.S.W;
anticipating that they would strike a rich gold-
mine with an inexhaustible lead of heiresses.
They were 12 in number, but provided accommo-
dation for a great many more, and, to do So. got
into debt to the extent of over £lO,OOO. This did
not trouble them at the time; as they reckoned
they would be. able to pay it all off slick, out bf
the “ dowries ” of the rich girls they were going
to catch. in that, however, they calculated
without their host. It was an easy thing to pro-
vide for new nuns, but it was quite another thing
to get them. And this was where the
Cardinal did not come in. Every Carmelite
is required to bring her dowry into the
concern, and those who lack dbwer need
not apply. A Cardinal who regarded the
Useless and morbid nunnery system with a favour-
ing eye could, c f course, by a suggestion here and
there, introduce a good many world-weary young
ladies with coin to the fold ; but Cardinal Moran
isn’t built that way, and the result is that,
although the Carmelites have been in Sydney be-
tween five and six years, they have, we are glad
to kn:w, been successful in bagging only one Aus-
tralian. They are consequently in debt. One
fat heiress would wipe out the deficit, and smooth
the path of the Carmelites for the future. But
the Cardinal,looks askance, and, for the presen
at any rate, “heiresses is hoff !”
* # *
Although Alderman Clark, “ merchant
tailor,” was Unanimously elected Mayor of Bris-
bane, his nomination for that office seems to be a
pure fluke, for as Brisbane is just now going
through the worst commercial year it has eve.
faced, other aldermen were reluctant to stand.
“ Blue Ribbon ” Clark has thus achieved tlie
second landing in his visioned staircase of fame ;
his next is to attempt to become M.L.A. for For-
titude Valley, or Nundah. Before the Griffith-
MTlwraith coalition, and when the Griffith party
was “solid,” it was understood-that John A.
Clark had the nomination of the then powerful
Sir Sam for the Valley seat; but now political
parties in Queensland are hopelessly mixed, and
will be perhaps hopelessly reconstructed. Still,
the reformed social reformer dreams on. As
commensurate with his new dignities, he has taken
a big house at Teneriffe, famous for the excessive
hospitality of “ the Wilsons,” and rips around
Brisbane in a buggy. As Clark must remain
loyal to his Good Templar’s oath —“not to sup-
ply, or cause to be supply to any person,” any sort
of “ pizen ” —his Mayoral allowance of £6OO ought
to see him through his year of office and leave a
small balance for ’oss-feed and ’armony.
# * *
One of the first mayoral acts of Blue-
Ribbon Clark, of Brisbane, was to employ a
labourer to carry away the grog “remnants ’’ left
by his predecessor in the mayor’s room. The
mayoral bin is, we believe, now stuffed full of
hop-beer, nosypop, and other mysteries. Mayor
Clark is, however, a terrific smoker, and, since his
aldermanic sun rose, largely favours cigars. Once
he forswore baccy for a year and a day, and pre-
sented a reporter with his pipe, as a token that he
was in deadly earnest. Next morning the re-
porter met him wearing in his mouth a weed as
big as a belaying pin.

Thus one of America’s minor poets :
Low on Helena’s neck toward the shoulder
Lurks the daintiest mole among the lilies ;
Feeding there on the sweetest roots, no wonder
Softer, sleeker he is than other creatures.
* * #
At Sydney Natatorium (swimming-baths)
—the waters whereof are sacred to ladies on
Monday nights—a very kittenish damsel put in
an appearance immediately after the expiration of
the males’ bathing-term, and incidentally gathered
that the men had just left. “Oh! is that soV’
exclaimed Miss Kitten, indignantly, “they have
just been swimming here, have they ! * Well,
then, I won’t risk going into the water, and I
won’t come here again, and I won’t allow any of
ray young lady-friends to come here. There now!”
And out she flounced.
# # *
The trial of engine-driver Phelan for a
Melbourne murder, was illumined by just one
little gleam of artless humour. A female friend
of the unfortunate wretch was put into the box to
testify that she had seen him “look eccentric ” on
a recent occasion. This reminds us that no jury
would be likely to hang ’Enry Parkes after taking
a good “ liker ” at his ’ead.
* # #
Several ladies are now making a good in-
come in London as professional face-washers. It
is claimed that the average society female has not
the faintest idea how to wash her face, and con-
sequently her countenance accumulates, in a
manner of speaking, and once a fortnight or so the
professional attendant comes round and gets up
arrears. According to one prominent operator, it
takes a good deal of heavy shovelling, too. The
patient’s features are washed for half an-hour in
twelve different waters, and then the lighter
shades of the picture are filled in with cold cream,
and after that the countenance is massaged for
another half-hour, and when the massage is over
there is some more washing, and the sufferer is
dried with a soft silk handkerchief. The pro-
ceedings conclude by the professor of cleanliness
slapping her client vigorously to promote circula-
tion, and if she also chased her for two miles with
a hoe in an uphill direction in order to strengthen
the muscles, and then chased her home again to
create an appetite, the treatment would leave
nothing to be desired.
# # *
The English papers are telling a joyous
story about a small, effeminate-looking burglar
who, finding that he was badly wanted by the
police, disguised himself in female attire, and got
a situation as lady’s-maid. He was an affable
and lady-like burglar, and though he was rather
awkward at first he speedily became invaluable,
especially at hair-dressing and similar personal
services. Then the police got on his track, and
when the lady-like girl, who had been treated
almost as one of the family, was run in under the
name of Bill Smith, his employer and her two
daughters made three separate and individual
back-somersaults, and went into frantic hysterics
on the floor. They have advertised for a new
maid since then, and in the advertisement they
state that any woman who isn’t provided with
references testifying that she is a female needn’t
apply. When they take down their back hair at
night they do it with a shudder, for they remember
how, for two dreadful years, that back hair was
taken down for them by a burglar ; and the same
lawless housebreaker helped them to dress for
dinner, and they were wakened every morning by
a coarse and brutal, malefactor ; and when they
were unable to sleep they used to ring the bell for
a robber attd get him to read to them. And not
only do they know these things, but all their
friends kttow them too, and talk about them ; and
when they see two horrid females laughing to-
gether in a ball-room, they feel as if three separate
oysters were walking up and down their delicate
spines.
* * *
An Adelaide man, sued by an undertaker
for the funeral-expenses of his defunct wife,
pleaded “not indebted,” calmly claiming that,
though the law insisted on his maintaining a live
wife, it did not compel him to bury a dead one.
He took his stand on the marriage ceremony which
enjoined on him (he said) a contract that only held
good “till death us do part,” and argued that his
wife’s demise ended his worldly interest in her,
and that he owed no obligations towards her
corpse. He had to pay.

It is rumoured that a very ancient C.M.G.
of poetic leanings Will shortly marry a young
Ballarat lady holding a Melbourne University
degree. We hope that this isn’t a result of “ the
higher education of women.”

TOMMY: “I want to say something, Pa.”
PA ( sternly ): “Be careful, rash boy. There’s nothing I’d punish you for so severely as
the utterance of anything suitable for illustration,”
and German soldier, stripped to their shirts and
trousers, cooking a friendly meal over a gipsy fire
and feeding the flames with their cast-off accou-
trements, rifles, &c. They might even appear in
the act of commencing to remove their martial
breeches. Such a work as that, so full cf feeling
and brotherly love, would take Berlin by storm
and restore the entente cordiale in a week.
* # *
Notice posted on the walls of every room
in one of the leading Calcutta hotels :—“All bills
should be paid on presentation, and, to avoid any
unpleasantness, residents are requested not to
remove their luggage until they have paid their
hotel bills and obtained a door pass, without which
the luggage will be detained by the door-keeper.”
* # ' *
A Maoriland paper reports that the hair-
less, irresponsible red offspring of the Governor
“is a true Onslow in face, with the dark, lustrous
eyes of the Gardiner family.” Some day, when
the world straightens out a little, that excited*
infant may find the spade of the gardener family
a very valuable as a means of making a livelihood.
# # *
Now and again Providence ordains that a
solicitor shall commit suicide out of respect for
the myriads of self-assassinated clients whose
bones lie bleaching in the cold ground.
# * *
The wife of at least one of the Federation
Delegates used to swear “something ’orrid ” at
the old man and kids in her young days. But
now she dances in the vice-regal quadrille, and
has toned down a good deal. In fact, she’s as
right as ninepence until you tread on her train.
* * *
The only thing Myles M‘Rae was ever
notable for in Parliament was his laugh. His is
constructed on the laughing-jackass principle, but
it undulates in the most remarkable manner. It
would be invaluable to a Wild West show.
M‘Rae is known to his parliamentary associates
as the “ member for posterity.” There is now an
added significance in the title.
# * *
Peterswald, S.A. Commissioner of Police,
has threatened that if any of his officers are found
through negligence, to have allowed Newbury,
the defaulter, to slip away, a strict enquiry will
be held. This proclamation, considering the
rumours current about the matter being “squared,
is most delicious.

Overheard ; “ May I have the next
waltz?” “I’m so tired.” “Then may I sit
down and chat to you.” “No, I think I’d prefer
to dance.”

From a lady’s letter from Sydney in the
Australasian, February 14, 1891: —
The liride was given away by her father, who wove a
short white walking dress, composed of muslin and em-
broidery, the bodice being made quite simply with a yoke
and fastened at the waist With a band of white ribbon,
bands of ribbon falling at the back of the skirt; small
white fancy straw hat trimmed with White heather, and a
white tulle veil.
The old matt must have looked really well; but we
wonder how the bride was dressed;

* * *
Frank Lincoln could tell a wicked little
story of how he volunteered to select a ttew
servant at a time when Mrs. L. was ill, and unable
to attend to sttch matters. “ I picked a regular
beauty down at the Registry Office;” said I rank,
“ but she didn’t stay long. About the third day,
1 enquired for the new girl, and the wife said she
didn’t think the ttew girl would suit, so
had sent her off with a Week’s wages. That was
all. The wife just looked hard at me, in a calm,
angelic sort of way and I said of course she knew
best. And she did know best.”

Mr. Nagel, who was brought out at great
expense from Europe to manage the colossal
“Australia” Hotel, Sydney, has resigned his
appointment and is returning to Europe.

A divorce barrister has been much exer-
cised by an application from a lady client. He
told her, as the first difficulty, that her case for
divorce would be heard by twelve sinners. “Then
again, my dear madam, you have wilfully deprived
your husband of your society, and when they hear
that, and see you, they will be dead against you.”
We read in ■ Sydney Echo that “Lord
Leigh has watched Sir Henry Parkes’career for
years and years with the greatest interest and
pleasure .” Some people are easily pleased.
“Wolfe’s Schnapps—look for £1 orders.
Sir John Robertson declined to attend the
Federation Convention dinner. “ Federation,”
says Jack, being obviously intended to ruin
N.S.W., he will have no hand or part in it.
The NiS.W. Freetrade Liberal Associa-
tion have passed a resolution affirming that “ in
all proposals respecting the Federal Constitution
the principles of Freetrade should be carefully
conserved and facilities afforded for their true and
complete development.
Mr. Cox, M.P., has sailed for Australia on
behalf of the Justin McCarthy section of the Irish
party. He will meet with failure in any effort he
may make to get funds, unless, indeed, he should
be patronised by the Imperialists with the sinister
purpose of strengthening the M‘Carthy faction
and thus permanently breaking up and destroying
the independence of the Home Rule party. On
the other hand, John Redmond, who comes on
behalf of Parnell, will receive an enthusiastic
welcome from the Irish in Australia.
A Sydney paper points out that “ not
until there is reasonable expectation of profit will
capitalists engage in the extension of existing in-
dustries or embark in new enterprises, and until
they do so there can be no revival in business and
no increase in the avenues of employment.”
Indeed ! Meanwhile, what do they live on ?
Capital or bank-interest? If the latter, and the
odds are 100 to 1 on, then they give just as much
employment to labour as under any other circum-
stances.

DIPSO.
A Yank, has patented a typewriter to hold
on the knee. Blonde or brunette ?
Bradlaugh died while preparing for the
press a book on “ Law and Labour.”
From being a publican’s champion in the
press, Jack Conway, of cricket and football fame,
has became a Boniface himself.
The Jews of California have bought a
million Mexican acres for the transplantation of
the persecuted Jews of Russia. Don’t think this
will work well. The Jews haven’t done much farm-
ing since the days of Boaz, and dog won’t eat dog.
They’ll need to import about ten times as many
Gentiles to get a living for them.
A London woman, with ten children still
surviving, who had buried her husband, two
children, and mother during the year, glided into
the undertaker’s shop at Christmas and timidly
asked him for a Christmas-box, because “the
family were such good customers.” The tender-
hearted undertaker kindly gave her a small coffin
in the rough.
A Sydney illustrated paper, more or less
rim by the ingenious Bennett family, prints a
couple of borrowed pictures, entitled “ Pater-
familias Revising the Morning Paper,!’ to which
are appended the following “gags:”—(l) “The
trash that newspapers do print nowadays ! I must
cut these scandalous things out before my daughter
sees them.” (2) “Great Scott! I declare there
isn’t a single thing left except advertisements!”
Morning paper in this connection is excellent.
For chilled-steel cheek that illustrated paper
stands without a rival.
As easy for the camel to go through a
needle’s eye, as to keep a newspaper going on
lines of sympathy for the working classes. So it
is found with the London Star, founded by T. P.
O’Connor, as Radical, but sold by him for £15,000
to a brass-and-clay image—half Radical, half not.
Now the Radical element has cleared out, so that
the paper remains in the hands of Mustard-maker
Colrnan Secundus, a gold-bug who entertains the
Prince of Wales at his magnificent place in
Norfolk. Starting a newspaper is like starting a
bank, always the felt want to be supplied, the
poor and struggling trader to be accommodated,
but at last the new establishment drops into the
swim, and goes for the cock-salmon of the big
accounts.
Says Pentecost in the Nineteenth Cen-
tury :
I have no sympathy with the outcry against Mr. Par-
nell on account of the discovery of his relations to Mrs.
O’Shea, not that I do not consider him fairly open to
adverse criticism and grave censure, but because I believe
that for the English people, or any large part of them, to
single out the Irish leader for their abhorrence on account
of such an offence, is peculiarly inconsistent and hypo-
critical. Ido not desire to excuse his folly. lam merely
pointing out the Phariseeism of the present English out-
cry against him. The very people who profess to abhor
Mr. Parnell's fault bow with reverence before Albert
Edward, beside whom Mr. Parnell is purity itself.
