Says the Twentieth Century :
General Booth’s scheme is merely a charitable enter-
prise, which would not change, to the extent of an iota,
the present conditions by which paupers and drunkards
and harlots are manufactured a hundred times faster than
he could reclaim them if his scheme should prove suc-
cessful beyond his wildest dream of hope. It would leave
the Land Lord and the Lend Lord as powerful as now.
• . . Booth means well, but he is only one of the great
army of philanthropists who are interfering with the
natural action of present social forces, and contributing
to prevent their natural consequences, and hence he is
doing harm when he means to do good. All charitable
or purely so-called philanthropic organisations should lie
abandoned, in order that the land, money and trans-
portation monopolies should be left to do-their perfect
work of degradation, starvation and death. The people
would then more quickly learn the real causes of their
misery and would remove them.
Irrigation for New South Wales.
ITS ADVANTAGES AND PROSPECTS.
During the last year or two, more than ordinary
attention has been directed to the value of irriga-
tion on account of the immense success attaching
to the efforts of the Chaffey Brothers in Victoria.
In that the colony have devoted large funds to
the purposes, and numerous Trusts have been
formed to carry out the work of fertilising various
parts of the country. Yet, in N.8.W., nothing
whatever has been done by the Government
towards the improvement of the vast areas of land
throughout its borders which, given sufficient
moisture, is stated by experts to be equal to any
wheat and fruit-producing country in the world.
This supineness may, indeed, be characterised as
almost criminal, when the immense advantages to
be gained by the system are taken into considera-
tion, and it is borne in mind what an enormous
population could be settled on country now
populated only by sheep and rabbits. Not only,
however, is there a direct disadvantage to the
farming population by the absence of any general
scheme or provision for the introduction of a
Government system. Private enterprise is stunted
and capital which, if it were possible to have it
invested without the worry and expense of passing
a special Bill through Parliament, is directed to
other channels. If there were even some pro-
vision by which irrigation on a systematic scale,
and under certain restrictions, might be carried
out privately, very much might be clone. But, as
matters now stand, any attempt to introduce the
system by corporations or individuals meets with
difficulties at the outset, inasmuch as a Bill has to
be introduced, and after much vexatious and ex-
pensive delay, it is often eventually shelved, and
there is the risk that from interested motives it
may be thrown out altogether. This has to a
certain extent been the experience of the
Land Company of Australasia, who may be
ranked amongst the pioneers of the move-
ment in this colony. During the last
session of Parliament, a Bill was introduced by
Mr. Abigail proposing to give the Company
power to conserve a portion of the waters of the
Page and Hunter Rivers for the improvement of
their celebrated Segenhoe Estate in the Hunter
Valley district. This Bill, after being advanced
to some extent, was amongst the measures which
remained unpassed at the close of the session,
though it will be taken up at the same stage next
session
No finer opportunity could be given, perhaps,
than that afforded by the Company’s Segenhoe
Estate to test the advantages of irrigation on the
soil of this colony. It is estimated that some 9000
or 10,000 acres of land on the Estate are eminently
suitable for agricultural and orchard purposes,
the only desideratum wanting to success being
an efficient water supply. The soil itself is
generally a beautiful, thick, volcanic loam,
which shows in some places on the banks
of the river to a depth of nearly 30 feet, and
an analysis of its constituent properties in-
dicates that it is capable of producing marvellous
quantities of cereals and fruit. In the latter
respect, perhaps, the land is scarcely equal to that
on the Company’s famous Ravensworth Estate,
the character of which is such that fruit-trees
develop there marvellously owing to a natural
system of underground irrigation, caused by the
shingly bed and water-flow under the loamy sub-
soil. Further notice will, however, be directed to
this property in a future issue. On the Segenhoe
Estate, on the other hand, the soil is in itself far
richer, and it needs but the one element—water—
to make it capable of producing unlimited crops.
It is comprised largely of wide flat reaches—
similar to that depicted in the photograph repro-
duced above —apparently formed in days long
past, by the action of flood-water from the higher
lands, which has brought down the soil and
deposited it on the level country below. Nowa-
days, however, no such thing as a flood
is ” known, and the land remains, no longer
disturbed by water, which, in fact, is required
in moderate quantities to develope its fertility.
The estate is situated in the Hunter Valley,
perhaps the most valuable stretch of country in
the colony. Within its borders is the junction of
the rivers Page and Hunter, so that, in addition
to wonderfully rich soil, ttiere is plenty of water
to hand for irrigation (now flowing as waste
into the ocean) which but requires the artificial aid
of man to turn it to account as a fertilising agency.
Altogether, the estate comprises 26,000 acres, and
prior to becoming the property of the Company
it was owned by the late Hon. James White.
That its fattening properties have been well
proved is shown by the fact that for 50 years past
it has carried an average of 4500 head of cattle.
Altogether, about 5600 bullocks are turned off
the estate every year, and these are mostly
purchased by Mr. Ben. Richards, the well-
known carcase butcher. Situated close to
the main trunk railway from Brisbane to
Sydney, it possesses groat advantages also in the
way of facilities of transit, as it commands inland
markets as well as those of Maitland, Newcastle
and Sydney. Already a considerable portion of
the land has been secured by small farmers, and
an area of 350 acres has been purchased by the
Australian Chilling and Freezing Meat Company,
who are erecting thereon works which will add
greatly to the value of the surrounding property,
and form the centre of a busy industry in the
district. The same Company has also another
large block under offer. Ploughing and clearing
operations have been coffimenced on the land
which has been taken up, and it is to be sincerely
hoped that this is but the forerunner of very
many cases in which great and at present un-
reproductive blocks of land will, under good
management and by the aid of irrigation, carry
a large and thriving population of prosperous
yeomen. The enterprising and practical managing
directors of the Company, Messrs. Edwards,
Walker and Harris, take the widest interest in
the work of making its properties thoroughly
reproductive, and as soon as the authority in the
Bill now before Parliament has been granted,
steps will be taken to secure similar powers in
connection with other estates. The Company is
certainly deserving of every success in its efforts
to introduce a system which should revolutionise
the future progress of the colony, and multiply
its resources and population.
The Ballarat Gas Company is content to
reduce the rate after paying a modest dividend of
7h per cent.
Maoriland, N.S. Wales, and Tasmania
supply, in the order named, the “Grand Old
Men ” of the Federation Convention, who may bo
thus tallied off—Sir George Grey, 79 years old ;
Sir Henry Parkes and Adye Douglas, nearly 7b
years. Parkes is four days older in “time ” than
Douglas, and a century or two more ancient in
“no savee.” After the septuagenarians limp at a
big distance Henry Cuthbort and Nick Fitz-
gerald, of Victoria, both 62 years. All the
Premiers of Australia are among the dele-
gates, but Maoriland’s chief Minister is absent.
Sir EL Parkes is the oldest Premier, and Sir S.
Griffith, of Queensland, the youngest, the
latter being 40 years old. The others
range—J. Munro (Vic.), 59; P. O. Fysh
(Tas.), 56; and T. Playford (S.A.), 51. John
Forrest's exact ago has not been disclosed, but it
is somewhere in the “roaring forties.” The
youngest delegate is Alfred Deakin (Vie.), who is
but 35, about one-fifth of which he has put in as a
Minister. Parkes ties John Downer for the ugli-
ness championship, and runs a dead-heat with his
colleague Toby Barton as to biggest size in hats.
Honest Tom Playford, of Adelaide, distances all
the others in largeness of boots, Colonel Smith,
of Ballarat, a near second. “ ChafFey ” Baker, of
Adelaide, ties John Donaldson, of Queensland,
for best-looking honours, which is meagre praise
at best. “ Oratorical ” Rutledge, of Queensland,
is the most pious; Munro, of Victoria, the most
intemperate teetotaller ; and the best capacity for
whisky is enjoyed by—well, a certain small man can
besafely backed to put under the table any one of the
remaining 44. There are, among the 45 delegates.
16 Australian-born members, thus made up—s
natives of N.S. W., 4 of South Australia, 3 of Tas-
mania, and 2 apiece of Victoria and West Aus-
tralia. Of the others, 12 were born in England,
8 in Ireland, 6 in Scotland, 1 in Wales, 1 in the
Isle of Man, and 1 in Lisbon (Sir George Grey).
The biggest man in the Convention is a Corn-
stalk (J. P. Abbott) and the shortest in stature,
though not in rotundity a Crow-eater Sir
John Bray. The introduction of flogging in
Victoria is represented by “ RigUeous” Wrixon,
who sits against the express wishes of the
present Premier of that province, and re-
presents the dead and buried Coalition of last
year. Among the delegates are 18 lawyers of
various sorts ; 8 squatters or quasi-squatters ; 6
representatives of mixed commerce ; 2 auctioneers
and land-sharks pure and simple ; 2 pressmen; 2
miners ; 2 civil engineers ; 2 old army men and 1
‘naval officer; 2 ex-parsons ; 1 each—farmer,
doctor, grocer, sawmiller, and surveyor; and 2
who can be called nothing but professional politi-
cians (Parkes and Lee Steere). In tiro category
of avocations, some members are counted more
than once ; thus, Rutledge counts both as an ex-
parson and as a lawyer; and Sir Thos. Mblwraith
as a civil engineer and a squatter. Duncan Gillies
is the confirmed bachelor of the Convention.
UNIMPEACHABLE TESTIMONY.
Eaots are established by testimony. This is so,
not only in laws of business and right, but also in
social life. But while the importance Pf evidence
is thus everywhere acknowledged, its weight is
purely dependent upon the character of the
witnesses. Unimpeachable testimony is therefore
what is wanted; Upon this principle the follow-
ing names are presented to the public as being
those of parties who, having experienced or

ments :

WATERBURY WATCHES at the
WORLD’S FAIR.
IN THE ENEMY’S CAMP.
Mr. D. Young 1 , of London, who is manager of
the Waterbury Watch Co.’s exhibit, informed me
that ho is telling on an average 500 Watches a
day in the industrial section of the Children's
i’alace, where the Waterbury is also on display;
and I was informed at the Waterbury- head-
quarters, in the Rue Montmartre, where a store
for the sale of Waterbury Watches exclusively
lias been open for some time, that the watch is
much liked by the French people, and the sales
are rapidly increasing.— Chameerlynk, in the
Manufacturing Jewel lek.
NOTE.—The local agents of the Waterbury
Watch Co. desire to draw the attention of would-
be purchasers of genuine Waterbury watches to
the fact that the word “Waterbury” in small
letters, and also the interwoven letters “W. W.
C 0.,” appear upon the dial face of every watch
manufactured by the abovenamed company. This
caution is, unfortuna ely, rendered necessary from
the fact that it has come under notice that numer-
ous persons have (much to their disgust) been
deceived hit) buying watches as Waterburys,
which were no more Waterbury watches than the
moon is green cheese. The agents of the company
will be glad to have any future cases of such im-
position promptly brought under their notice,

Notice to Artistic and Literary Contributors
Strictly Original matter contributed to The Bulletin
will be paid for. The Editor will be (jlad to receive and
consider contributions as under :—
1. Original humorous or political matter, illustrated or
other wise. (Drawings must be made in clean lines
of Indian ink on white paper or Bristol-board.)
3. Unpublished anecdotes of living celebrities, or people
who ought to be celebrated.
3. Humorous poems (from eight to forty-eight lines pre-
ferred ).
l>. Serious poems, of similar or greater length, especially
those suitable for pictorial illustration,
o. Short Australian or other stories, up to, say, 3000
words. About half that length, or even less, will
be preferred.
All Contributions must be Written Legibly, on One
Side Only of the paper, and folded, not rolled. 2 he
name and address of the writer must appear, in all
cases, on the MS. itself, and not mereh on an accompany-
ing letter or paper. The Proprietors do not hold them-
selves responsible for Rejected Contributions; but, if
accompanied by a stamped and addressed envelope or
wrapper, every effort wrill be made to return them.
Ihe Editor does not undertake to enter into any
correspondence respecting rejected Manuscripts.

England in 1891.
A. London paper thus discourses on “The Bright
New Year”:—
The country at large was perhaps never in worse plight
than it is to-day. Proofs gather hourly that agriculture
in England is slowly decaying, and that with its fall the
labourers wages (when he gets any) are equally narrow-
mg to absolute starvation figures. Villages that 20 years
ago were prosperous, and whose shopkeepers were well-
to-do, are now deserted or being deserted ; one little
st ° r ? V^ es for those who are left > and a glance at the
omcial Gazette would show that even the keeper of that
store is working with goods obtained on credit and with
property covered by bill of sale. The fanner cannot
grow wheat to compete with America or India, and (lie
course of his education has been such that he does not
understand that he might, at any rate, keep his head
above water if he only knew how to raise hogs, butter, or
poultry. His mental vision embraces the Hall and the
Ghuich, and that is all. They did for his father and they
are good enough for him. If he has to bankrupt and the
squire or the parson seizes every chattel he possesses-
Well, it is the will of God ” and nothing can prevent it.
The silly ass neglects to think that when he was a boy
wheat was 00s. a quarter as against half that figure now—
that land worth 30s. an acre per annum then is worth
much less than 15s. now, although he still pays the 30s.
His only book is his Bible, and he ignores farming litera-
ture altogether; Slowly the land is reverting to ils
owner, who himself cannot work it from want of capital
Or knowledge. The land-hunger from which his ancestors
suffered so much, and which has resulted in the possession
of what he would now like almost to give away, drags i.im
down and dow’m The Hall that once boasted a score of
Spruce servants has now only a slatternly brace or so, and
the stables hold but a pony; The shooting or fishing is
let to a London capitalist or Manchester manufacturer
and on the rent the family practically lives. ;
Pait of population is actually d\i 11 g (or, what is
Horse, starving) from want of even a potato. . , ;
Countless thousands of railway-hands, dock labourers,
any labouring class you like, are on strike, or constantly
Coming out on strike. 1\ by ? Simply because the st rikers
want food for their families. Tney don’t mind working
for it, but they must have food. 1891, any night we like,
say 12 o’clock, with a thermometer at 29 below freezing
wall find some 70,000 to 80,000 frail women on the street.-
of London alone-many of them well-born and bred, but
forced by stern necessity to ply the calling. Perhaps
another half-million or so do ditto, but not p’-buclv.
Scarce any of this army hut would be virtuous vv.e it not
for La toe—laws which permit the capitalist to suck the
life-blood out of the worker Then we are
told by the level-headed Tom Mann, that at least
1 ;9,0C0 men are out of work. As we write, we
read of a woman found on London Biidge, cur busiest
thoroughfare, frozen to death, and without a morsel of
food in her stomach. The “ bright New Year” opens ns
to t ie city or business generally with a ghastly vista of
horrors . . . shows a myriad of pale, wan faces of
Irstwlule investors whose savings have disapiOmd for
ivei 11 the maelstrom of swindling joint-stock companies
. . . shows broken homes strewn across (he land
... does not forget a long list of suicides . . .
miows an ever-lengthening list of bankrupt retail trades-
men, cf shops shutting or shut up, of once well-to-do
bread-winners reduced to penury 1891
means coming death. Does the ‘“bright New’ Year ”
bring happiness, then, to none ? O, yes, to capitalists, and
ail who slaver in the train of royalty, aristocracy and
religions. These welcome 1891; it may give them still
Uloie sinecures, more interest on their investments, move
fat livings, more superstitious idiots from whom cash can
be extracted. 1891 is a splendid time for such as these-
things never were so cheap. Luxuries may be obtained
for nothing, almost, and meat is down a penny a pound
M ires dresses or hats can be obtained wholesale ; a man’s
labour can be got for eighteenpence a day, a woman’s
1 i oaf °f£ read - • • • Everything connected
H-ith Capital must have a gay old time. What, then, dees
Capital care? , . . Let Labour starve. , . . What
matters the army on the street—lost in perdition for ever
- provided the Queen gets her £500,000 to £l,OOO 000
p -r annum, and the bishops and clcrcy their reir’iG,--
stipends? 6

Personal Items.
Douglas B. W. &c. Sladen has settled in
New York.
The late Dr. J. D. Lang christened one of
his sons “ Cape Horn.”
Young Baronet Tichborne is about to
Australia with his wife.
Three of Coach-Proprietor Cobb’s ex-
drivers are now Australian millionaires.
Judge M‘Farland (N.S.W.) is going
“ home” to publish his book on Australia.
B. J. Fink has, it is said, £lOO,OOO worth
of shares in M‘Cracken’s City Brewery Co.
(Melb.).
Ihe only man in Sydney in the profes-
sion who does not look like a detective is the
Frenchman Rochaix.
Dan O’Connor says that the daughters of
Governor Jersey are “the loveliest cratures he
ever set eyes upon. ”
Probate lias been granted in London to
the will of late T. Brown, merchant, of Sydney.
Value, £599,318 sterling.
Mr. F. B. Suttor, M.L.C., attended the
Bathurst banquet to his brother W. H. Suttor
M.L.C. This means a lot.
Mr. Ernest Brodribb, the Titanic plunger,
sold his carriages and horses the other day, pre-
paratory to leaving Melbourne.
Sir George Grey, delegate from Maoriland
at the Federal Conference, landed as Governor of
South Australia 50 years ago next May.
Rev. Dr. Woolls, E.L.S., the eminent
scientist, of Parramatta, has turned into his 78th
year. He has been 60 years in Australia.
It was most incautious of Postmaster-
General O’Connor to say that anyone who yells
at a public meeting is an “uncultured beast.”
How glad Mr. Justice Foster must be that
he lias escaped being a member of such an immoral
body as the present N.S.W. Legislative Assembly!
John Dillon, now serving a six-months’
sentence under the Irish Crimes Act, is in the
gaol hospital. He is an obviously consumptive
man.
Alf. Deakin has “ done ” Indian irrigation
in 40 days. Felix Tanner lived on nothing but
irrigation for 40 days. Deakin will live on his lot
for a year.
It was Mrs. M‘Rae who was returned fo r
Morpeth, N.S.W., not her wicked husband, Myles.
It will be .Myles, however, who will be called
upon to resign.
The Law Times (E.) calls for the retire-
ment of Justice Stephen on the grounds of senility
and eccentricity. These are no disqualifications
in the N.S.W. District Court.
The ex-Bathurst town clerk would appear
to have made his little alleged “ grab ” in the last
ten days of holding office—just after a couple of
aldermen had said how honest and good he was.
Last week Mr. Sydney Smith, N.S.W.
Minister of Mines, said that Sir Henry Parkes
was the greatest man in Australia. This week,
Mr. Sydney Smith will please note, Sir George
Grey is in Australia.
Governor Kintore alleged, at a banquet
the other day, that he was commissioned by the
Imperial Government to make the trip across the
continent, as they want to know why that part of
Australia isn’t developed.
' Lord Hindlip, of the British Beerage, is
visiting Australia for the benefit of his health.
His lordship has been suffering from an attack of
disappointed shareholders in “ Allsopp’s,” who
want half-a-million of their purchase-money back.
It is rumoured that Sir Henry Parkes has
at last become wealthy, but rumour does not say
by what means. However, certain persons who
consider that “ honor does consist in paying 20s.
in the £ ” will be glad to hear of the old man’s
good fortune.
Playing whist at Marlborough House the
Ozar was informed that he had “revoked,” and
said it was strange—he had never done so before.
“I expect sir, you have often, but no one liked to
tell you of it,” replied a bluff Scotchman—Mac-
kenzie of Kintail.
“ General” Booth, according to Commis-
sioner Ooombes, arrives in Australia next Sep-
tember. It is stated that South Australia is to
be one of the places for the “ oversea colonies ”
mentioned in the scheme. A S.A. landowner has
offered the Army 20,000 acres, and the offer will
be accepted.
The Adelaide papers report that Governor
Kintore (whose wife, by the way, is just now
absent from Australia) has sent his vice-regal
photo, to an elderly lady in the Benevolent
Asylum, It is to be hoped that his intentions are
strictly honourable.
Huxley declares that a grammar of In-
fidelity may be patched up from Cardinal New-
man’s writings. Newman was as liberal as a
Catholic can be. Pope Pius wouldn’t have
him at any price, but Leo gave him the red hat at
once. His theology came close to Victor Hugo’s
Will—“ I believe in God.”
“ Lord Carington has sent out by the mail
to all his old friends in Australia splendidly-
executed three-quarter length lithographs of him-
self and Lady Carington, with his and his wife’s
signature at the foot, and a note begging of their
acceptance.” Lithographs ! The showman stick
here sticks out a mile —as usual.
Gordon-Cumming’s announced intention
to submit his slander-actions to a public tribunal
instead of to arbitration tends to the belief that
he is innocent, and that the Prince of Wales and
Co. are on the wrong side. If Wales persists in
exerting his influence to keep the matter out of
court, people will know what to think.
It is stated that J. G. Jenkins, M.L.A.,
will be appointed Minister of Education in South
Australia, vacant through Bew’s death. lie was
until lately Adelaide representative of the Pic-
turesque Atlas Co., is an ex-book-canvasser, and
possesses a Yankee twang. If Jenkins is ap-
pointed it will be the first good thing that ever
happened to a book-canvasser.
While in Tonga (where he only stayed for
a few hours) R. L. Stevenson the novelist paraded
up and down the beach in a white skull-cap and
drew his coat about him complaining of the cold.
“ It’s lovely,” he said to a resident, “but I cannot
bear the cold.” Meanwhile the indigenous sav-
ages were making a bad attempt to keep cool,
each in a mat fan and a pair of garters,
Amid all the talk of work in the London
slums of darkest England, we may recall a dis-
tinguished man who died in Australia, years ago,
Edward Denison, who gave up a position as
member of parliament to live and toil among the
London poor. Denison is universally acknow-
ledged the model of unselfishness, power, amd
success. There is a certain rule about not letting
your left hand know what your right does, but
only the wicked practice it nowadays.
The late William M‘Kinley, law-reporter
for the Melbourne Argus, once was told by a then
junior barrister that he (the J. 8.) despaired of
making a living in Melbourne, and_ intended to
try the country. “Your chance is certain to
come; don’t ruin your career by going into the
country,” advised M’Kinley. The young bar-
rister changed his mind, and that is how Grace
Sumner, in the recent notorious Moss will case,
came to be cross-examined by J. L. Purves, Q.G.
The recently-deceased Mr. David Bews,
Minister for Education in South Australia, was a
newspaper-proprietor. It was he who lately re-
marked in a speech that he didn’t think he was
nearly so happy in his prosperity as when he was
a boy looking after hogs at half-a-crown a week.
Possibly when lie began to associate with poli-
ticians and individuals of that sort, he realised
for the first time how much he had lost in quitting
the society of pigs.
The American Law Courts have decided
that the Duchess of Marlborough in future will
have to maintain her position Oh £16,000 a year
instead of the £26,000 she declares indispensable.
Which reminds us of a Mrs. C—a N.S.W.
woman who not long ago went “home.” “ When
he went to England before,” she said to a friend,
“he had only £40,090 a year, and we found our-
selves cramped for money at times, but now we
have £60,000 a year and shall be able to live quite
comfortably.”
MR. WM. ALEX. SMITH, F.S.S.
Secretary of the Citizens’ Life Assurance Company.
Young Earl Dudley, it is said, is to marry
Maud, second daughter of the Prince of Wales.
The family history of the Guelphs is sufficiently
scrofulous, and the world is not unaware that
Earl Dudley is the son of a father whose leading
delusions were that he was about to add to the
population of the world, and who used to make a
nest under the impression that he was hatching
eggs. The Bulletin prays that the blessing of
Heaven may continue to rest on the young couple
who start life under such favourable auspices.
Sir Edward Clarke, the English Solicitor-
General, is a self-made man of the Good-Stories-
for-Little-Boys pattern. The sole joy of his
mother —and she was a widow, &c. —little Edward
used to say, “ Mother, when I’m a big boy I shall
study for the law,” and then he would kneel at
her feet, and fold his little hands together and
pray, “ 0, Lord, let me sit on the Woolsack before
I die.” And it begins to look as though the Lord
means to let him. Clark is a clever lawyer, and
one of the political fathers of “ Conservative
working-men.”
M‘Donald, ex-crier, DO years in Mel-
bourne Supreme Conrt, is in ter viewable, on his
afternoon stroll. He thinks the generation of
Purves, Madden, Topp, Isaacs, and Co. im-
measurably inferior to that of Follows, Ireland,
Sewell, Michie, Dawson, Aspinall, and Billing,
Mac’s old associate was Lowry, and a young
reporter went up for the first time to the sessions,
when he Was on duty. He took Lowry for the
prisoner, up for stealing a watch, and wrote a
pathetic description of an elderly nan of most
respectable exterior, clad in rusty black, with a
white tie.
Says “America” :
A convict named Muir, from Glasgow, who is the
original of Marcus Clarke’s “ Barrington, the Prince of
Pickpockets,” was the author of the celebrated phrase :
“ We left our country for our country’s good.”
This is a garbled quotation from a description in
London Athen.uum of Marcus Clarke’s prose
writings. Muir, of Glasgow, a gentleman of
standing, was one of a party of Scotch political
martyrs, whose sufferings and adventures are as
omantic as anything ever told in a novel, while
Barrington, the alleged author of the famous pro-
logue and quoted line, was simply a clever
scoundrel of whose existence even Muir probably
was unaware. Muir was a really noble man.
Bishop Kennion, of Adelaide, asks the Pre-
sident of the Wesleyan Conference to excuse him
from taking part in the Wesleyan centenary cele-
brations, on the ground that he would be com-
pelled to express his conviction that modern
Wesleyanism has departed very widely from the
position taken up by John Wesley, and that “ he
would not willingly go out of his way to say a
word to hurt the feelings of his brother Chris-
tians ” •
He wound “ brother Christians? ” Kind Heaven
forbid it !
Yet, saying he’d never insult ’em— he did it!
Rank folly in honesty’s nothing to credit,
Avowing we never would say it— ive’ve said it!
Wrote Oswald Keating©, “D.D.,” who
died last week at Darlinghurst prison under such
tragic circumstances, in a recent article in the
Sydney Quarterly Magazine “In this age
money is the great God of the world. Money
will make wrong right, crime virtue, black white,
and the worse appear the better reason.” True ;
and the application of the apothegm to Keat-
■inge himself lay in this: that had he been pos-
sessed of enough money and little enough vanity
to have been defended by an acute barrister,
instead of standing up in Court to defend, or
rather to Convict himself, no jury could possibly
have found him guilty on the evidence submitted.
Logically regarded, it was a very strong case of sus-
picion, and nothing more. It was not good
enough to hang a dog upon.
Old proprietor W , of the C J
newspaper, lately summoned his editor to the
proprietorial sanctum:—“Mr. S , I have no
complaint to make of the articles you supply,
which are good enough. But sometimes, when
you have taken too much drink, you make use of
such frightful language that I am really afraid of
retaining you in my employ for fear you might be
the means of losing me my character.” Ed. :
“Then, in case your apprehension should be
realised, you should consider me your friend.”
W : “What do you mean?” Editor (beginning
to take his coat off): “Mean? Why, that you
have the character of being the meanest ill
the town, and shquld I be the means of losing yoll
that character, you ought to consider me the best
(adjective) friend you ever had.”
David Syme at one time—long before he
became a howling Imperialist and said Australia
would have to help England in keeping Russia
out of India—determined on dropping in the
Melbourne Age all recognition of “colonial”
military titles when dealing with subjects in
mufti, so to speak. Thus, volunteer - Major
Cheesemould would become, in the report of a
purely civil function, plain Mister Cheesemould
and Captain de Wellington Boots would be sim-
ply Mr. Boots, and so on. This plan, however,
elicited the disapprobation of no less a military
amateur than the great Colonel S , of big-
buots notoriety, and he remonstrated with David
about not being paraded in print with his military
title. “ But what’s your objection, Mister
H——asked David, dulcetly, “ to being called
‘Mister?’ What’s your object in wanting an
honorary colonial shoddyism flung at you every
time?” “Well, you see,” said the Colonel,
“ there are so many S s that if people get into
the habit of dropping the ‘ Colonel ’ everybody
will be calling me ‘W. C. S “ Well?’'
queried Syme. “Well !” replied the Colonel.
There is little chance of defaulting bank
manager Bradley being caught. The description
given in the £5OO-“ reward ” advertisement omits
all mention of his halo.
Mrs. Spencer, the oldest colonist of
Western Australia, died last week. She arrived
per the ship Rockingham in 1830, at which time
the colony numbered 850 inhabitants.
The lectures and speeches of that charming
talker, the late Dr. Badham, of Sydney Univer-
sity, are being collected by his old pupil, Mr.
Thomas Butler—now Professor of Latin at the
University—and will shortly be published by sub-
scription.
The high-water mark of extravagance at
the Amphitryon Club, London, where they sell
dishes at 10 and 15 guineas apiece, writes the cor-
respondent of a Melbourne paper, was not reached
until a night or two ago, when young Lord Dud-
ley gave a “short” dinner to four friends, for
which the bill was £9B odd.
Mrs. Maybrick will yet break qut. She
has a strong sort of Tichborne party ; her Whal-
leys and Onslows are desperately energetic.
Arsenic was the trouble, found on her dressing-
gown, like the tell-tale aroma of Countess Zicka’s
scent on the stolen documents, in “Diplomacy.”
As soon as the rope was taken off her fair white
throat, imprisonment for life only meant months.
If out of sorts try Wolfe’s Schnapps.
We are informed by a correspondent that
there was no rowdyism or disorder at Clermont (Q.)
when the “ blacklegs ” arrived, only a desire on
the part of the unionists to have an opportunity
of dissuading the arrivals from fighting the battle
of monopoly. It seems that the Mayor, also
editor of the local paper, refused the use of the
Corporation boat to convey supplies to the Union
Camp. ,
Use Batho’s Baking Powder.
It is cabled that a petition has been made
for the removal from the English Bench of Justice
Sir James Eitzjames Stephen on the ground of
insanity. He was very hard-headed when he
tried Mrs. Maybrick lately. He is famed for his
heterodoxy, and his book on “ Liberty, Equality,
and Fraternity,” while as a lawyer his feat was
going to India and emulating Macaulay's work, in
codifying. Hard-headed and red-headed, one
somehow seems to want evidence.

“THE BULLETIN.”
(Published at SYDNEY, N.S.W.)
The Unique Weekly—Australia’s Nonpareil
Illustrated Newspaper.
“The Bulletin ”
Premier Australian Literary Journal,
Is the only paper which has a consistent and dis-
tinctive Australian National Policy.
The Bulletin favours—
A Republican Form of Government*
Payment of Members.
One Person, one Vote.
State Revenue derived directly from the Land.
Complete Secularisation of State Education.
Reform of the Criminal Code and Prison
System.
A United Australia and Protection against
the World.
The Bulletin denounces—
Religious Interference in Politics.
Foreign Titles.
The Chinese.
Imperial Federation.
The public eye rejects as uninteresting more
than half of what appears in the newspapers of
the day. It is only the remainder that is found
in The Bulletin.
The Bulletin is an aggressive Democratic
paper which strives to exclude from its artisti-
cally-condensed columns all matter which is hot
of general human interest. The Bulletin was
started eleven years ago with no capital but brains>
and has become a vast property, because it pbs.
sesses a vitality lacked by the countless news-
paper-ventures in which Australian capitalists
have unavailingly spent large fortunes*
The Bulletin’s conductors claim that every
progressive paper on the Australian continent has
more or less endeavoured to profit by its example
and follow its lines. The result of its powerful
influence and its unprecedented literary and coill-
mercial success can at Once be seen by comparing
the methods pursued by the Australian daily and
weekly press prior to and immediately after the
first issue of The Bulletin.
From the date of The Bulletin's inaugural
publication its success Was assured. It had at
once a dozen more or less servile imitators, many
of which even took, so far as was possible, its
name. They are now all dead, while The Bul-
letin continues the one phenomenal success in
Australian journalism, the one completely non-
local and distinctly universal Australasian news-
paper.
The Bulletin proprietary have spent spine
thousands of pounds in fitting their buildings
with the latest appliances for high-class printing
and the production of illustrations, and they have
now issued The Bulletin in an entirely new
form—printed with new type of improved legi-
bility on the finest paper, and adorned with
sketches from the hands of the ablest and best-
paid newspaper artists Australia has ever seen.
The Bulletin is the oidy well-established Aus-
tralian journal which, throughout its career, has
been consistently Radical. It points with pride
to the fact that for years it stood alone among
secular newspapers in advocating the Irish
National Cause, and that it was the only Aus-
tralian journal which denounced the Soudan
Expedition from its inception.
The Bulletin is the beacon-fire of national
progress : it is the forceful summons to the
crusade against the monopolies in land, wealth,
power, and privilege—the direct cause of half the
misery which now afflicts British humanity.
Against the claims of avaricious clergy who claim
to monopolise salvation, against the dark despotism
of grasping plutocrats, and against the exercise of
any human authority which has not been granted
by the people over whom it is exercised, The
Bulletin will fearlessly and ruthlessly contend.
ThE Bulletin, unlike the ordinary weekly
journal, is not loaded with a weight of matter
transferred from the columns of a daily paper-
issued from the same office. The Bulletin is
not connected with any other paper, and the
matter it contains is all specially prepared by its
own unequalled staff.
The Bulletin, which is the only paper sold
by runners in the streets of every Australasian
capital, is the most widely-circulated of all papers
published south of the equator, and is the only
Australasian publication which enjoys a popu-
larity unhampered by the political boundaries
that so closely restrict other journals to the
colonies in which they are printed.
The Bulletin’s red cover is equally familiar
to the bushman of the Far North, the stockman
of Central Australia, the pearl-sheller of Torres
Straits, and the digger in the New Zealand
Ranges. A paper which is at once the most
popular city publication and the organ of the
intelligent bushman must indeed be broadly based
Perhaps the strongest proof of The Bulletin’s
popularity is found in the unchallengeable fact—
easily ascertainable from any postal official—that,
of all journals issued in Australasia, The Bul-
letin is the one most frequently remailed from
one reader to another.
The Bulletin is the journal which, of all Aus-
tralian journals, is most frequently quoted in Eng-
land, America, and the colonies.
The Bulletin is going ahead faster than any
other paper, because its vast mass of original and
specially-written reading matter is the brightest
and best, its illustrations the cleverest and most
artistic, and its policy the boldest and most
straightforward ever submitted to the Australian
public.

Victorian Racing.
With fine weather and the promise of good sport
in their favour tfie V.R.O. people had to lament a distinct
falling in Saturday’s attendance at Flemington. Some
previous Newmarkets have drawn a bigger crowd and ex-
cited far more speculation at the post, the scarcity of the
better sort of local punters—the steady-going individuals
who have a pound or two bn every race at big meetings,
but are never seen “following the sport” at small ones—
being a significant sign of the times. It is something
more than dullness of trade front which the absentees are
suffering. They have been dropping their money per-
sistently for a long time, and just now they are swearing
off racing. The last Melbourne Cup-week, in spite of
Carbine’s win, wAs a real bad time for the majority of com-
htercial punters, who “ went down ” on the minor handi-
caps, as they always do when N.S.W. horses or any horses
not trained and touted within five miles of Melbourne,
are getting more than a fair share of the good things. So
it happened that neither the bookmakers in the paddock,
nor the nobler animals in the Birdcage were furiously
mobbed on Bungebah’s day.
* # #
No shout of joy rent the air as the big horse
lobbed in all by himself. In Victoria he was first favourite
only in a professional way of speaking, for the general
public didn’t get wind of the “dead bird ” until his price
was too short to tempt them. Most of the local trainers,
jockeys, and representative knowing ones backed Bunge-
bah for a bit, but nine-tenths of the ring-money was
scooped by a small Sydney crowd. There were four other
betting races (Carbine’s canter not counting), and as they
all went to N.S.W. horses, the Melbourne victims of their
home-bred fancies returned home in a pensive mood. In
truth, winners want too much finding since James White
died, and the outside public are growing weary of trying
to pick ’em in the dark.
# * *
Before the Leger was run Gibraltar looked like
the horse that couldn’t win, consequently, it is hardly
fair to sneer at the touts who had arrived at one wrong
conclusion on the subject.
* # *
The A. J.C, Derby winner was certainly not as
hard in appearance as The Admiral, Enuc, and others,
but, being healthy enough inside, his extra pluck and
length of stride brought him home. Gibraltar, probably,
is the only game animal Of the lot (barring Correze, who
seemed tucked up and overdone), but as fine condition is
absolutely essential in a 21-mile race, it seems very im-
probable—writing before the event —that he can win the
Australian Cup. If Enuc, with only 7st. Mb. to cany,
hasn’t, by the time this appears in print, beaten all the
other Leger horses his failure can be put down to a con-
trary spirit. The Ballarat colt could have finished pretty
close to Gibraltar and Admiral on Saturday but for Power
easing him. Yet he seemed more glad of the excuse to
“ turn it up” than any real four-legged gentleman ought
to be.
The Federation Convention banquet—
whoo-whoop ! Try Marshall’s Tonic Nervine.
Premier Munro has alluded to Duncan
Gillies as his “ noble predecessor.” Rise, Sir
Duncan, elder sunken.
“ A country must be an exalted one that
pays a beast and a coward £6 a week to go run-
ning about seducing young girls and blackening
his wife’s eyes.’’—Cumberland Argus.
They did not let Dan O’Connor “spake ”
at the Federation banquet. They only allowed
him to say “Hear, hear,” which he did all the
time.
Lord Jersey : —“ Let history teach a
nobler lesson to mankind, and show how the bonds
of union can be drawn closer, and show that the
history of the old world shall never be repeated
here.” Hear, hear. No more Earls, no more
Monarchs.
When the Federation banqueters , had
drunk the special toast—“ One People, One
Destiny”—the band played “Rule Britannia.”
And fittingly so. “One People, One Destiny,”
as’ thus musically interpreted, is the Imperial
Federationist motto.
Paderewski, the great Polish pianist, comes to Aus-
tralia. The only successor of Liszt in dashing play ; a
marvel as an executant.
One dose of Salt Regal makes you feel a’new
man. After several you would not know yourself
again;
“We desire no alteration from the happy
conditions of Australian life,” said Banquet-Presi.
dent Parkes. Who are “we”? Ask the labour-
unions of Sydney and Melbourne and Adelaide ;
enquire of the shearers of Queensland, and the
miners of Charters Towers, Newcastle, Bulli and
Broken Hill; question the seamen of Australia
and Maoriland ; poll the farmers and bushmen all
over the continent; and then report the answer.
Parkes declared at “the banquet” that
Australia’s ambition is (we quote the S.M.
Herald verbatim report.) “to remain side by
side with that dear old England we all love so
well .... to share all her difficulties, and
honours, and glories, and to be equal in every-
thing beneath the sway of the British Crown,
and under the beneficent rule of our Sovereign
Lady the Queen.” Yet this is the Parkes who
made Australian nationalistic political capital out
of his opposition to the despatch of the Soudan
contingent ! .
The accomplishment of Australian Federa,-
tion, unless a sudden outside danger should
menace us, which is an event most unlikely, to
happen except as a result of British pigheaded-
ness, will be a tortoise movement. It will be
lucky, indeed, if it be as sure as it will be slow.
Contrastingly, the after proclamation of an Aus-
tralian Republic, which is a certainty of the
future, will probably be a sudden and violent act.
Whatever resolutions the sitting Convention
adopts, its work can only be preliminary. It has
to be submitted first to each Australian Parliament,
and next, as Premier Playford has publicly
stated, to the public vote. If Australian Federation
is made the test-question of Parliamentary elec-
tions under the present electoral systems, the
sneak-plotters and Imperialists, by the agency
of their own particular infernal machine —
plurality of votes —will continue to debauch the
country, and will attempt to sell their adopted
land, bound hand and foot, into perpetual
liegedom.to a foreign crown. The principle of
One-Man-One-Vote should be statutorily intro-
duced in every province before its people are
asked to return or reject can didates on the Federa-
tion question ; or, this plain and only issue should
be decided by a plebiscite of the people, whose
personal vote should decide the result.
After fitting Victoria Regina with wings
and a halo. Premier Parkes, at Monday night’s
banquet, proceeded to butter Governor Jersey by
repeating ’Opetoun’s self-adulatory and chuckle-
headed remark about “a new departure in Gover-
nors.” “ The new Governors are a very different
set of people to the old lot of governors,”, echoes
Parkes. How, and why ? They have more money
perhaps, and they certainly have “higher” titles,
yet is Jersey or Carington a better or more dis-
tinguished man than, (say) Sir John Young, or
Sir Hercules Robinson? Is Lord Onslow, as a
man, an improvement on Sir George .Grey ?
“Greatness ” is Parkesianly estimated by Imperial
titles.
Capable judges are unanimously of opinion
that the Convention banquet oratory was utterly
unworthy of what was intended to be a great
occasion. The keynote of the whole dispiriting
performance was “ God Save the Queen,” and the
living inner fires of Australian N ationalism were
carefully banked under the dust of artful
generalities. The regiment of newspaper re-
porters to whose lot it fell to record the lip-drop-
pings of the after-dinner orators when asked to
give their candid private opinions of the proceed-
ings, almost unanimously voted the speeches flat
and inconsequent. Parkes was trickily earnest on
puerile points, but his language was cold and weak
and evasive—diluted and elusive—whenever it
pretended to touch the important subject that
forms the ostensible cause of the Convention. The
other speakers faithfully rivalled Parkes in
mediocrity. The shadows of the evening’s oratory
were traced in deepest indigo. Amid the mono-
tonous drivel, a commonplace platitude seemed a
sparkling crystal of inspired polish.
Set a bank-manager to catch a bank-
manager.
Premier Munro (at the Imperial Federa-
tion gorge) would “ not have faith in the existence
of a Supreme Being if we did not believe we were
sent here (to Australia) for a great purpose.”
Just so. Then Munro can excuse a blaekfellow
who disbelieves in God’s existence, seeing that
the argument flattens out the black man alto-
gether. And Munro wouldn’t have faith in build-
ing-societies if he had been one of the Premier
Permanent’s insolvent victims. Or in land-booms
if he wasn’t a boomer himself. Or in Parkes, if
he was a real Australian Nationalist, instead of a
canting Scotch pedlar.
Parkes, in concluding his response to the
toast of his health at the Imperial Federation
banquet, said that if he could help in bringing
about a United Australia, “ he would care for
nothing else!” Great is the power of humbug
to deceive .the listening masses—if people only
took Parkes at his own valuation they would
swallow his peroration' as gospel. Yet the same
speaker had, within the previous two hours, in the
course of several speeches, devoted all his oratorical
powers to the ignoble task of prostrating Australia
afresh at the foot of an old-world throne, and
renouncing, in the name of a half-awakened
nation, all present hopes of national inde-
pendence, besides beslavering the distant monarch
with the most fulsome personal flatteries, as if
she were an evergreen stage fairy, or an ever-
young ballet-girl, instead of a commonplace,
stout, hard-fisted, well-preserved old woman
who loafs on the forbearance and misery of the
poorest populace in the world, and accepts the
grovel and the proceeds of the robberies of the
richest oppressors that ever |eized and held public
plunder. Parkes’ speeches were all swayed by one
dominant idea—the- sacrifice of Australia on the
altar of the Empire ; the placing of its neck once
and for all under the iron-shod boot of a foreign
monarch. The Old-Man-of-the-Sea would hand
Australia over in manacles to the Old-Woman-
over-the-Sea. But Parkes has failed to gauge'the
Republican instincts of the Australian democracy
—the inner soul of our people. He has said : “No
power on earth can throw back the cause of Aus-
tralian Federation.” If any power could throw it
back,' it is Parkes’ cool attempt to condemn Aus-
tralia to the hideous slavery of perpetual vas-
salage. No power on earth can prevent the ulti-
mate triumph of the cause of Australian Federa-
tion in one Independent Australian Republic.

The Workman’s Alternative.
A Story of the Great Dock Strike.
I.
The currants wore drawn. The lire burned
brightly. A cheap shaded lamp shed its warm
glow of light on the miscellaneous crockery and
turned spoons which were set out for the evening
meal.
Lottie Hazell was sitting on the bright steel
fender, stirring something in a little saucepan on
the fire, and hushing on her knee a frail white
child, two or three years old. In spite of its
obvious poverty, and a certain stuffiness which in
so imtny minds seems indissolubly associated iVith
comfort, the room was a cheerful one, and had a
pleasant, welcoming ait about it —an air not lost
on its master when, having stumbled up the ill-
lighted staircase, he opened the badly-hung dobr
of his home.
The boy—he could not have been more than ifi—
came in whistling gaily, kissed his wife, and sat
down in the ricketty Windsor chair opposite the
hearth. She looked up at him with reproach in
her eyes.
“ You’re late, Bill.”
“ Yes, old gal, I’ve had a queer day —an un-
common queer day. How’s the kid ?”
“ Poorly, very poorly. I took her round to the
dispensary to-night, and he says ‘ Beef-tea ?’ ”
Hazell bent his brows.
“ It’s all very well, as I told him-,” the wife wetit
on, “ but gravy beef’s not to be had for the ask-
ing-. Howsomevet, we must gO without some-
thing more ourselves. It seems like as if it was
all ‘going without-.’”
A deepet line between Bill’s brow's had by this
time crossed out tho eager and excited look with
which he had entered tire.room.
“ It’s damned bad luck. I say, old girl, don't
take on. It'll be all for the best. We shall pull
through somehow.”
She rose and stood facing him, the child in her
arms, its little bloodless hand hanging helplessly.
“'What do you mean, .Bill,” she said, “Some-
thing's up. What is it ? ”
He rubbed his head atid looked at her doubt-
fully.
“Now don't you [rut yourself about, Lottie-.
You’ll see it will be all right.”
“ God bless the man, what is it I ”
He tdse with a pbbr assumption bf Its being
iVothmg, went over to the shaky wash-hand stand
and dipped Iris head in the tib basin. His voice
came shaky through the splashing water, “ We’re
out on strike ! ”
11.
A misty, rainy morning in November. The
great paved space of Tower Hill was dotted over
with groups of men—twos and threes together
standing talking, discussing the future of the
great Dockers’ Strike. So far all had gotie well,
and private'privations seemed little iii the flush
of a great iithusiasm—little to men accustomed,
-week in, week out, to a life of privation. There
is something int’bxieatmg in the mere fact of being
banded together with one’s fe low-men for the
attainment of any common end—an intoxication
of which your mere individualist knows nothing.
The excitement of a great aim, something immea-
surably beyond the dull round of their work*a-
day lives—filled these men; and the talk among
the groups was hopeful, bright, enthus as tie.
Bill Hazell was among them—thinrier than a
fortnight before —but with a keener light in his
eyes. His voice was among the loudest raised in
discussion aird talk, and face bore on it most
strongly the seal of determination. His was the
loudest “ hurrah ” when the well-known straw-
hat of the leader of the strike was seen at last.
* * *
“He’s the man to pull a thing off,” Bill
s lid to his companion, as the leader appeared in
the road, which, running above Tower Hill, seems
a sort of vantage-ground or platform from which
one can speak to a crowd.
The leader pushed forward. A nod here, a
handshake there, a volume of prosiness cut short
and answered by a word—and he sprang on the
parapet and waved the straw-hat in answer to the
triumphant acclaim of the crowd. Then he began
his speech, his daily speech, from which the dock-
labourers learned What were the hopes of victory,
what the threats of defeat. What matter what
he said ? He knew how to speak straight from his
heart to the hearts of the men who heard him.
He checked, encouraged, reproved, stimulated,
passed on his own enthusiasm and faith, and,
abore all, he always looked like winning. He
told them what money had been sent by the
workers of other countries to help them in their
hour of need. He told them what he had done
and what other men had done on their behalf
since the previous day. He told them what would
be done by him and them that day to further the
cause they all had at heart. Then, amid ringing
Sheet's atid a fit© of enthusiasm that even k London
tain was powerless tb damp, the crowd dissolved;
dispersed, and, bannered and banded, the proces-
sion of t|ie penniless started on its daily march
through the richest city of the world.
Bill was of it: He held one of the cords bf a
banner, a position of responsibility that lightened
the fatigue bf his long hour’s march. The roads
were muddy with threb days’ rainfall; and boots
in the army of labour Are seldom nluch to boast of.
Even scarcely interested passers-by, glancing
casually at that procession with its firm step, its
ha gat'd, resolved faces, and the bra. e light in the
dockers’ eyes, said to themselves, “These men
nleau Winning, and they will win;”
Bill had no dinner that day, unless the slice Of
bread which his rear-rank man shared with him
can be called a dinner, and he was sick with
hunget as he olilbbed the stairs to the little room
which held hiS wife atid child.
lit.
As he entered the room his wife sprang to meet
him, and flung her arms round his neck.
, “ Thank God you’ve come home, Bill,”she said
hoarsely; “she’s worse.”
“ Who’s worse? The child,” he said, but not in
the tone of asking a question.
“ Yes —the child, our little girl as you said you
loved dearer than me.”
“ I only said it to tease you, my girl; ” he held
her with his arm-, and stroked her with a sort of
rough gentleness. “ Let’s have a look at the kid
- then a bit of supper. I’m wore out, Lottie, old
woman-. ”
She lighted the candle, and the father and
mother stood beside the bed where the child lay-
wasted and thin. Any doctor could have told
them that the child was weakly—could never
live to grow up, but doctors are not given to plain
speaking. To them it seem d that if the baby
could only be better fed, she might grow round
and rosy, like the children who placed on the
flags of the court outside —children of parents a
little higher in the social scale than a dock-
labourer, children energetic enough to “ play at
strikes” with an old tin tray and a few bits of
rag for flags. The two stood looking at the child.
Bill broke the silence in answer to something un-
spoken.
“Poor little kid! I can’t help it, Lottie ; I
can’t help it. I give you everything I get —x'dief-
tickets, grocery-tickets, and all. I can't help it.
What’s a chap to do ? ”
* * *
She turned on him almost fiercely.
“ Do? Why chuck up this cursed strike. Go
back to work. You’re sure of work now—and
better money.”
“ Go back to work,” he repeated slowly.
“ Look here,” she went on, “ look at our home.
It wasn’t much afore, but look at it now. Where’s
our sticks? Where’s our duds? Where’s every-
thing ? Gone t > pay for your holiday for you to
go processioning about all day long, while me and
the child may stay at home and starve for all you
care.”
Bill sat down on the edge of the bed.
“ You knock me all of a heap, Lottie,” he said,
“you do indeed. I told you what the strike
meant, and you raid—”
“ 1 don’t care what I said.”
“ But unless we stand by each other, as Burns
was saying to-day, the strike’s no good, and all
this goes for nothing.”
“ One won’t make any difference to them,” she
hotly answered, walking heavily across the room
and leaning her elbows on the mantel-piece.
“ But suppose everyone says that, tire strike
wou’d be over,” and he rubbed his rough chin with
his hand.
“Never mind what eVeryone says: What
you’ve got to think of is me and baby.”
“ Don’t I think ol yOU?” ho cried with sudden
violence, getting off the bed and beginning to walk
up and down the room. “God bless - and save the
woman ! What else do you suppose I think of
when tramping about rill day on an efnpty
stomach ? It’s fdr the sake of bur wives and kids
that we strike for better money ; Jack said So this
ttiorfling.”
“ H ang Jack !” Then she hesitated a moment;
and came across the roan t'd him.
* # *
{ ‘ Bill,” said she, “ have I Been a good wifb
to you, or haven’t I ?”
“ ’Course you have, old Woman, don’t take on sd
now.”
“ Then, Bill, I beg and pray yoti bn my knbes
tb drop this strike and go back to work and —save
our little girl. Oh, Bill, what does it matter
what you do? You ain’t everything to the strike;
but we , haven’t nothing but you—me and baby
haven’t.”
She had actually flung herself down on the floor
as she spoke. He stooped to lift her.
“No,” she cried wildly, “not till you say yes.
0, Bill, for God’s sake—there’s nothing left to
pawn—them grocery-tickets is no good for getting
her what she wants —say yes.”
He did raise her then and set her on the wooden
box that served as a chair.
“I Can’t, my girl,” he said; “I can’t go back
on my mates. It woilld be a damned dirty trick.
I can’t do it.”
There was a long silence, broken only by the
sound of a street-fight below.
Bill tried to return tb the commonp' ace*
“'Got any supper for me, old girl? I’m wore
out.”
“No,” said Lottie, bitterly, “ I have no supper
for you, nor for me neither; nor no breakfast nor
dinner. I sold the ticket for tuppence to get beef-
tea for baby, and I’ve had nothing all day.” Her
voice faltered her —-“Oh, Bill, I’m so hungry.
Won’t you go back to work? I could’borrow
threepence from the people downstairs this minute
if you’d promise.”
“My poor old woman,” he answered drearily,
“ I can’t do it. I couldn’t never look a mate
in the eye again. I can’t —not if we all starve
for it.”
“ May God forgive you, then ; I can’t.”
And at the bitter cry the child woke, w r ailing,
weakly and pitifully. The mothers arms were
round her in a moment.
“And she’s hungry again, now. My God,
what can I do ?”
IV.
“ End of strike. All men to return to work on
Monday morning. By order of the Council.”
That notice was posted up outside headquarters
on Saturday evening, but Bill Hazell did not see
it. He was with his wife, who sat on the straw
that served them for a bed, with a baby in her
arms. They fed the child with milk, but she took
little, and lay mostly sleeping in her mother’s
arms. When she was awake her mother sang to
her the gayest songs she could recall —sang them
with an unmanageable lump in her throat, an
unspeakable weight at her heart.
. .... 0
On Sunday morning Bill went out.
“ Bully finish, eh ?” said a mate who met hind
near Tower Hill.
“What?”
“ Strike’s Over; We’ve won. Last procession
starts at ten., You must be in it.”
Bill turned and ran back along the streets;
bright with the autumn sunshine. He wotild not
lose a moment ni telling Lottie the neivs. Ah !
poor girl, how sad she had looked an Hour beforb
when he had left her.
4 ‘ The strike’s oVer, my girl,” he brird, “and
tVe'Ve won.”
His, wife was still sitting oh the bed with thb
child in her arms. She turned and looked at hint
coldly, without speaking.
Bill flung , his old hat up iii an uhcontrollable
excess of triumph—All hardships and struggles
forgotten in victory;
“We’ve won—by God!” he said. “A grand
(piece of work !”
“Hush,” said his wife, still not moving. *‘Hush
—baby is dead;”

An Idyll of the Plains.
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
Thk morning after an “a’coholically wet night”
is always a bad experience, even when fairly
good liquor has been consumed, and a bath,
and what is called “a morning,” will set you
nearly right. But the morning after a night of
bush-shanty rum and case-brandy—such brandy !
—of brands unknown in civilised parts, is an ex-
perience that is simply a foretaste of the orthodox
hell.
If hell consists of the veriest loathing of oneself,
of a confused memory of riding impossible races,
fighting impossible fights, and playing impossible
games of cards, accompanied by a wearied stiff-
ness and soreness all over the body, and a raging
thirst that only a lake or lagoon could satisfy;
then, one need scarcely die to experience it. So
dimly thought Tom Rawlings as he awoke from
a drunken stupor on the boarded verandah
of a remote bush pub. in the Far West,
where he had passed a few hours reposing
with his head on his saddle and still unstrapped
swag. He had only arrived the evening before, so
that as yet the poison had not had time to get
him thoroughly under control, and he was able to
sit up and try to think.
It was sunrise; for, in point of fact, the flies
had awakened him, and his first idea was naturally
to allay the thirst by which he was tormented.
Looking around, with an uncertain notion of where
he was, or how he had got there, he indistinctly
made out that not far off there was a water-bag
hanging from a rafter of the verandah, and by its
side, a pannikin.
This meant olysium, and Tom staggered to his
feet; made for the bag, and 1 failed, holding him-
self up by the verandah-post. He took the
pannikin and dipped it into the bag. It was
nearly a third full of water that had been
hanging there all night, deliciously cold.
He lifted the pannikin to his lips, but
the. first few mouthfuls seemed to pass
down his throat untasted. Then, as his
tongue and palate became slaked the coolness made
itself felt and he hastily gulped down the re-
mainder and dipped down for another supply, A
third followed, for real “outside” stingo rum
takes a deal of quenching, and then he paused
and had a look around.
The early morning is always beautiful in Aus-
tralia. There is perhaps only one place to which
its softness cannot lend a charm, and that is a
stony ridge in the interior, among the old-man
spinifex country. But, even in the depth of
summer in the laud of the great plains, or repel-
, lant sand-hills, the hour after dawn has a poetry
of its own thatlqnds a spell to the very desert.
Tom recognisecHtiothing of this, he was trying
to collect his scattered thoughts, and recall the
events of the past night.
His money !
He felt in his pouch and pockets. Empty, of
course, but that he had expected He had two or
, three cheques in his swag were they safe ? The
cold water had cleared his head, and after treating
himself to another half-pint he went over to his
saddle and examined the swag strapped on it.
That was .evidently untampered with —he knew
the holes he had pulled the straps to and his own
peciWiar trick of"tucking the ends in. Better to,
leave everything alone for the present until ho
had straightened himself up a little more.
Someone was moving in the bar and Tom went
in. The landlord, who never seemed to go to
sleep, was there, stooping behind the bar, pouring
something out of a wicker-covered jar into a stone-
ware keg. He rose up on Tom’s entrance, with
rather a red face.
“Ullo, Tom ! you up already, old man ?”
His tone was kindly, so that Tom instinctively '
knew his cheques were safe as yet.
“Yes. What the deuce did you let me sleep on
the verandah for? I’m as stiff as a horse.”
“Let you sleep there! How could I help it
when you would sleep there, spite of anything I
could do?”
“Well, show us a room now,” said Tom.
The landlord bustled round the bar. Tom had
just finished a large fencing-contract on a station
fifty miles to the westward, and was pretty sure
to have a big cheque on him.
Mine host of the Halfway Inn prided himself
on being a severely honest man, who always gave
his customers what he considered full value for
their money. He never stuck up drinks that a
man had not called for; but then, if a man was so
drunk that he kept on calling for drinks without
partaking of them, why, that was only fair busi-
ness. And down they went.
Tom went out to the verandah and carried his
saddle and other impedimenta into the rude bed-
room assigned him. The landlord was too wily
to press him to drink ; he knew that a man on the
first morning is full of good resolutions, and would
sternly reject such overtures. He bided his time.
Tom sluiced bis face and head, and as he dried
himself he looked out of the window. Not far
away was the rough horse and cow yard, and a
small black boy, clothed only in a shirt, below
which his legs appeared like two thin black
sticks, was putting the three milkers in. A
young woman with buckets in her hands stood by,
waiting for the slow-moving beasts to step over
the sliprails. ,
Tom suddenly thought of a drop of fresh milk,
mainly, it must be confessed, in connection with
rum. He knew who the girl was—an orphan
niece of the landlord, who had lately come to live
at the place.
Tom went to the bar, got a large glass, and in
it some so-called rum, and strolled across to the
yard.
By this time one of the milky mothers had been
bailed up, and the girl, with her head down, was
busy at her task. She did not look up for some
time, and then coloured a little at seeing Tom
watching her. She was a comely, fresh-coloured
girl from the uplands of Naneroo, and Tom
thought that it was the pleasantest woman’s face
he had seen for some time. But then he had
been living in the western back-blocks ’for many
years.
“ Did you want some milk?” she stammered.
It is always the woman who finds her tongue first.
“ If you please,” said Tom in his best manner.
She took a pannikin from the empty bucket and
dipping it into the fresh, frothing milk, filled the
glass he held out.
As I said before, a real old fighting rum-thirst
takes a lot of quenching, and Tom drank off the
glass of milk without drawing breath. If the
cold water in the parly morning had been distilled
nectar, this was something doubly distilled.
Every 'Cobweb wad cleared from his brain, and
Tom felt as “ fit” as he had done four-and-twenty
hours previously, ‘ere he drew rein at the Half-
way Inn.
He stood. watching the girl at her work, the
fresh kine-like smell of the new milk bringing
back to him strange thoughts of his boyhood’s days
on the far-away Clarence.
As for her, the presence of a rather iowzled and
red-eyed bushman was not sufficiently a novelty
to make her feel bashful, but for some unaccount-
able reason she felt shy under his fixed regard.
Evidently he had “ been on the burst,” but then,
according to her limited experience, most bush-
men did go on the burst occasionally. He was a
fine, broad-shouldered, strong fellow —very strong,
she concluded —and she liked strong men.
By and bye old Snowy gave a vicious bang with
herxail, upon which he caught hold of it and held
it, Showing himself good-natured also. It is highly
disagreeable to get a bang in the face with a cow’s
tail when you are milking.
The country wooing of other lands has been the
theme of both poets and artists, but as yet the
loves of Australian Bush-lovers are unsung and
unpainted. True, the surroundings of Arcadia in
Australia are commonplace. Tennyson would
never have derived inspiration for that pretty
pastoral song of his that Queen Elizabeth is sup-
posed to hear the milkmaid singing :
Shame upon you, Robin,
Shame upon you now.
Kiss me, would you? with my hands
Milking the cow.
from such a scene as I am describing. But the
great and universal feeling is as strong in the salt
and blue bush country as it was in the days of
Queen Bess ; and when Tom, after waiting until
Hilly’s task was finished, carried the buckets up
for her, and she thanked him with another soft
flush, the magnetic spark of weal and woe had
been as surely kindled in the two, as if they had
been the veriest idlers in existence without a
thought beyond falling in love.
Tom’s attentions that day wore marked. To
this neither the uncle and aunt had any serious
objection, only the worst of it was that it inter-
fered with the more serious business of drinking.
Still they could make no remonstrance, Tom being
a man of his hands, and well liked in the district,
besides, there was always a chance of an outbreak,
and that bigeheque, which the landlord knew must
have a bod ily existence, and for which his soul hun-
gered and thirsted, might eventually come his
way.
Five days after Tom had had that drink of
now milk hi the cow-yard the fellows on Banjeban
station were surprised to see him riding up to the
place ho had left only a week before."
The usual snigger went round. “By jingo!
Tom didn't get far this time.”
“ Managed to stick to his horses, anyhow.”
“ Got a bottle in his shirt, I bet.”
“And 4 head,on him, too,” said another,
■ amidst a laugh.
But they were all wrong. Tom had neither a
“ head on him ” nor a bottle in his shirt. When
he got off his horse he looked ten per cent, better
than when he started, and a thrill of surprise
went through everybody.
Soon afterwards he sought an interview with
the manager. “ Have you let that bit of fencing
we were talking about, Mr. R - ?”
“ Not yet, Tom, you mean the short piece you
said it was not worth your while to take.”
‘4Well,” said Tom, “ITo changed my mind
and come back for it if you’ll give it me at the old
price.”
“Of course, but you’re not stone-broke yet,
Tom, are you?”
“ That doesn’t look like it. does it?” said Tom
grinning, as lie. produced the two cheques he had
received for former contracts. “ Will you look
those up for me, Mr. R. ?”
And now comes in the adventurous part, of
course. Milly is carried off by natives, such as only
exist in yellow-backed fiction, and is, of course,
rescued single-handed by Tom. Or a bush-fire,
such as never was lit in Australia, comes swooping
down, and as she is fainting before it Tom rushes
up on a coal-black steed and, with one herculean
grasp, lifts her on to the saddle and gallops away.
Nothing of the sort.
Tom quietly stuck to his contract, and when
the parson came round, rode over to the Halfway
Inn, and —of course he had arranged with Milly
beforehand-got married, and in process of time
shifted with his wife in‘o more settled regions and,
—I know no more. With matrimony ends the
idylk

Cheerful item from London Truth ;
It is fortunate that murderers are not chemists, for
there are poisons known to every chemist which leave
absolutely no trace behind them. One there is—and
this, perhaps, was used in Italy and France when poisoned
gloves were the fashion—which only has to he put in the
water in which a person washes his hands for him either
to die or to go mad a few months later, Then again,
what bunglers murderers are in getting rid of a corpse, if
they prefer steel or a bullet to poison ! There is an acid
in which you only have to immerse a corpse for it to be
dissolved'in about an hour. The water is then poured
away, and not a trace or a vestige of the body remains.
Putting aside the criminality, the foolish way in which
people commit murders is a reflection on human intel-
ligence.
Why didn’t a lot of people we know drop into
that acid when they were very young? Also how
much of it would be required to swamp out the
Imperial Institute, and the N.S.W. Freetrade
party, and the Orange Lodge, and the Social
Purity Society—any news about an acid which is
strong enough to dissolve gimlets will be thank-
fully received at this office, and the whole body of
O.M.G.’s and K. O.M.G.’s and G.C.M.G.’s and
the country J.P., and the great bulgy, representa-
tive Australian, and the hangman—gallows in-
cluded, and the Harmy—drum included; and is
there any reduction on taking a quantity ? Also
we want some reliable information about the fluid
which causes insanity when people wash their
hands in it, and we will be glad to receive tenders
for washing about 00 politicians. 111 Foreign-
trade editors, 200 preachers who discourse on the
joys of poverty, and 18,7f-0 assorted grovellers—
not because we want them to go mad, but because
they are obviously mad already, and a strong
chemical might bring them round. And if it
didn’t bring them round they could afterwards be
dropped into the other compound for there is a
holy joy in the thought of a fluid that will swallow
up a whole pawnbroker so that he can be after-
wards gathered up in a jug or fired through a
hydrant. And to dream of one’s mother-in-law
and all his creditors flowing softly down into a
crystal stream somewhere, to babble over the
pebbles, and his aunt breaking slowly on the
shore, and his tailor' rolling in with a gentle
murmur on the yellow sands is pleasure unutter-
able, and then to steal out mysteriously on a dark
night, and empty the landlord into the sea with a
quart pot, and - so on.

SAPOLIO
To clean tombstones.
To polish knives.
To clean dishes.
To renew oil-cloth.
To scrub floors.
To whiten marble.
To renovate paint.
To wash out sinks.

South Australia has only 48 newspapers?
or one to every 6000 of the population. There are
28 country papers, of Which only six appear more
than once a week.

Gaol-Chaplain Scott finds that the number
of b discharged prisoners” received into the
bosom of the Prison Gate Brigade of the Salva-
tion Army, in Melbourne, during last year,
exceeds the total number of prisoners released
from all the gaols in Victoria during the same
period ! ’
Apropos to the Plumpton benefit “scan-
dal,’ the scandal lay in the giving of any perform-
ance for the honour and profit of a man who has
no perceptible claim upon the theatrical profes-
sion, and certainly no claim upon the public. A
few Melbourne people know Plumpton as a musi-
cian and head of a little society of mutual admira-
tionists, or as the compiler of a w eakopera. Musical
people know him in those capacities, while the
vulgar crowd know him as the recipient of virtuous
invectives from a certain Evangelist. The term
“ benefit,” in its theatrical sense, is degraded by
association with such an utterly undeserving case.

Lord Kintdre is accused of issuing an order
that all his servants are in future to be clean-
shaved. It is lucky for Aide-de-Camp Thomson,
presuming that the order would have affected him,
that he became engaged before this order was
issued, as his “fetching” moustache presumably
played no inconsiderable a part in the capture of
that rich S.A. heiress.
One Jones, an ox-soldier, “ of exceptional
ability and high character,” was recently con
Victed and sentenced to 18 months’ gaol for
forging and uttering an army-form for £39, osteh-
sibly “ deferred pay ” to a corporal. Jones was
enployed as clerk in the Woolwich Pay-office at
2s. Gd. per day, or 3yd. an hour. His “ Queen
and country ” made him a felon almost by brute
force.
